1

Units & Measurement

⭐ NEET Weight: 1–2 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 1 | SI units, significant figures, dimensional analysis, error analysis

SI Base Units — The 7 Foundations
Read Detailed Explanation

The International System of Units (SI) forms the foundation of all physical measurements, defining 7 mutually independent base units: meter (length), kilogram (mass), second (time), ampere (current), kelvin (temperature), mole (amount of substance), and candela (luminous intensity). All other physical quantities are derived from these base foundations.

Base QuantitySI UnitSymbolDefinition / Key Point
LengthMetremDistance light travels in 1/299,792,458 s in vacuum
MassKilogramkgDefined via Planck's constant h = 6.626×10⁻³⁴ J·s (2018 revision)
TimeSeconds9,192,631,770 oscillations of Cs-133 atom
Electric currentAmpereADefined via elementary charge e = 1.602×10⁻¹⁹ C
TemperatureKelvinKDefined via Boltzmann constant k = 1.38×10⁻²³ J/K
Amount of substanceMolemol6.022×10²³ elementary entities (Avogadro number)
Luminous intensityCandelacdDefined via luminous efficacy of 540 THz radiation
Practice Problems For This Concept
Q1 · SI Units
Which of the following is NOT a fundamental SI unit?
a) Ampere
b) Candela
c) Newton
d) Kelvin
Newton is a derived unit (kg m/s²). The fundamental SI units are metre, kilogram, second, ampere, kelvin, mole, and candela.
Q2 · SI Units
The SI unit of solid angle is:
a) Radian
b) Steradian
c) Degree
d) Candela
Steradian is the SI unit for solid angle, while radian is for plane angle. Both are supplementary units.
Q3 · SI Units
Which fundamental unit is defined using the Planck constant?
a) Second
b) Metre
c) Kelvin
d) Kilogram
Since 2018, the kilogram is defined in terms of the Planck constant (h).
Q4 · SI Units
Light year is a unit of:
a) Distance
b) Time
c) Speed
d) Intensity
A light year is the distance light travels in one year in a vacuum. It is a unit of length.
Q5 · SI Units
One parsec is equal to:
a) 3.26 light years
b) 3.08 x 10^16 m
c) Both a and b
d) 1.496 x 10^11 m
1 parsec = 3.08 x 10^16 m, which is approximately 3.26 light years. Option C is more accurate if both were precisely matched, but b is the standard metric value.
Dimensional Formulas — Complete NEET Reference
Read Detailed Explanation

Dimensional formulas express derived physical quantities in terms of the fundamental dimensions (M, L, T, A, K, mol, cd). Enclosed in square brackets, they reveal how base quantities combine to form a derived quantity (e.g., Force = [MLT^-2]). The Principle of Homogeneity mandates that only quantities with identical dimensions can be added or subtracted.

Mechanics Dimensions

Force, Weight, Tension[MLT⁻²]F=ma
Energy, Work, Torque[ML²T⁻²]Same dimensions!
Power[ML²T⁻³]P=W/t
Pressure, Stress, Young's modulus[ML⁻¹T⁻²]F/Area
Momentum, Impulse[MLT⁻¹]Same dimensions!
Angular momentum, Planck's constant h[ML²T⁻¹]Very important!
Velocity, Speed[LT⁻¹]
Acceleration[LT⁻²]
Angular velocity (ω), frequency[T⁻¹]rad/s
Gravitational constant G[M⁻¹L³T⁻²]F = Gm₁m₂/r²
Surface tension[MT⁻²]Force/length
Coefficient of viscosity (η)[ML⁻¹T⁻¹]Pascal·second
Moment of inertia[ML²]I = mr²

Electricity & Magnetism Dimensions

Electric charge[AT]q=It
Electric potential (V), EMF[ML²T⁻³A⁻¹]W/q
Resistance (R)[ML²T⁻³A⁻²]V/I
Capacitance (C)[M⁻¹L⁻²T⁴A²]q/V
Inductance (L)[ML²T⁻²A⁻²]E=LI²/2
Magnetic field (B)[MT⁻²A⁻¹]F=qvB
Permittivity (ε₀)[M⁻¹L⁻³T⁴A²]C²N⁻¹m⁻²
Permeability (μ₀)[MLT⁻²A⁻²]TmA⁻¹
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

In Units & Measurement, practice questions often test the Principle of Dimensional Homogeneity or require converting units between different systems. A common conceptual trap is forgetting that dimensionless quantities (like strain or angle) can still have units (like radians). Always cross-check the dimensions of both sides of an equation to eliminate incorrect options quickly.

Q1 · Dimensions
The dimensional formula of angular momentum is the same as that of:
a) Linear momentum
b) Planck's constant
c) Energy
d) Power
Angular momentum L = mvr → [ML²T⁻¹]. Planck's constant h: E=hν → [ML²T⁻²]/[T⁻¹] = [ML²T⁻¹]. Same!
Q2 · Dimensions
Which of the following pairs have the SAME dimensional formula?
a) Force and power
b) Stress and pressure
c) Velocity and acceleration
d) Momentum and energy
Stress = Force/Area = [MLT⁻²]/[L²] = [ML⁻¹T⁻²]. Pressure = Force/Area = [ML⁻¹T⁻²]. Same!
Q3 · Dimensions
The dimensional formula for coefficient of viscosity is:
a) [ML⁻¹T⁻¹]
b) [ML²T⁻²]
c) [M⁻¹L²T⁻²]
d) [MLT⁻²]
From Stokes' Law, F = 6πηrv → η = F/(6πrv) = [MLT⁻²] / ([L][LT⁻¹]) = [ML⁻¹T⁻¹].
Q4 · Dimensions
The dimensional formula of gravitational constant (G) is:
a) [ML²T⁻²]
b) [MLT⁻²]
c) [M⁻¹L³T⁻²]
d) [M⁻²L³T⁻²]
F = Gm₁m₂/r² → G = Fr²/m₁m₂ = [MLT⁻²][L²]/[M²] = [M⁻¹L³T⁻²].
Q5 · Dimensions
Which of the following is a dimensionless quantity?
a) Gravitational constant
b) Planck's constant
c) Gas constant
d) Strain
Strain is the ratio of change in dimension to original dimension (e.g., ΔL/L). It has no units and no dimensions.
Dimensional Analysis — All 3 Applications
Read Detailed Explanation

Dimensional analysis has three primary applications: (1) converting physical quantities from one system of units to another, (2) checking the dimensional consistency of a given physical equation (Principle of Homogeneity), and (3) deriving the mathematical relation between different physical quantities by equating powers of fundamental dimensions. Note that it cannot determine dimensionless constants.

1. Checking Equation Correctness

Principle of Homogeneity: LHS and RHS must have identical dimensions. If dimensions don't match, equation is definitely wrong. If they match, equation may be correct (doesn't guarantee correctness).

2. Deriving Relations

Assume physical quantity depends on others as a product: Q = k·Aˣ·Bʸ·Cᶻ. Equate dimensions on both sides to find x, y, z. Limitation: cannot find dimensionless constant k.

3. Unit Conversion

Use: n₁u₁ = n₂u₂. Express u₁ in terms of u₂ using dimensional formula. Example: 1 J = 10⁷ erg (CGS). Convert by substituting base unit ratios.

Limitations of Dimensional Analysis

• Cannot find dimensionless constants (e.g., 2π in T=2π√(l/g), ½ in KE=½mv²)
• Cannot distinguish between quantities with same dimensions (e.g., Work and Torque both [ML²T⁻²])
• Arguments of trig, log, exponential functions must be dimensionless
• Cannot derive equations involving addition/subtraction (e.g., v=u+at can't be derived, only verified)

Practice Problems For This Concept
Q1 · Dim Analysis
Using dimensional analysis, which formula for the time period T of a simple pendulum is correct?
a) T = k√(l/g)
b) T = k√(g/l)
c) T = k·l/g
d) T = k·g/l
T ∝ lˣ·gʸ. [T] = [L]ˣ[LT⁻²]ʸ = L^(x+y) T^(-2y). -2y=1 → y=-½. x+y=0 → x=½. T = k√(l/g).
Q2 · Dim Analysis
If Force F, Velocity v, and Time T are taken as fundamental units, then the dimensions of Mass are:
a) [FvT⁻¹]
b) [FvT⁻²]
c) [Fv⁻¹T]
d) [Fv⁻¹T⁻¹]
F = ma = m(v/T) → m = F·T/v = [Fv⁻¹T].
Q3 · Dim Analysis
In the equation P = (a-t²)/bx, where P is pressure, x is distance and t is time, the dimensions of a/b are:
a) [MLT⁻²]
b) [MT⁻²]
c) [ML²T⁻²]
d) [LT⁻²]
a must have dimensions of t², so [a] = [T²]. P = [T²]/[bx] → [ML⁻¹T⁻²] = [T²] / ([b][L]). Thus [b] = [M⁻¹L⁻²T⁴]. [a/b] = [T²] / [M⁻¹L⁻²T⁴] = [ML²T⁻²]. Wait, a/b = Force? Let's check: Pressure x volume = Work. Pressure x distance = Force/Area x distance = Force / L... Correct answer is MT⁻²? Wait. [a]/[b] = [T²] / [M⁻¹L⁻²T⁴] = [ML²T⁻²] which is Energy. Wait, the options say MT⁻²! Actually, Force x Distance = Energy. The answer is [ML²T⁻²] but let's check P = a/bx. Let's fix option c.
Q4 · Dim Analysis
Which of the following equations is dimensionally correct? (s=displacement, v=velocity, a=acceleration, t=time)
a) s = vt²
b) s = at
c) v = at²
d) v² = 2as
v² = [LT⁻¹]² = [L²T⁻²]. 2as = [LT⁻²][L] = [L²T⁻²]. LHS = RHS.
Q5 · Dim Analysis
Dimensional analysis CANNOT be used to:
a) Find the value of dimensionless constants
b) Check the correctness of an equation
c) Convert units from one system to another
d) Derive relationships between physical quantities
Dimensional analysis only works with dimensions, so it is completely blind to dimensionless constants (like 2π, ½, etc).
Significant Figures — All Rules
Read Detailed Explanation

Significant figures represent the precision of a measurement, including all certain digits plus one doubtful digit. Key rules: All non-zero digits are significant. Zeros between non-zeros are significant. Leading zeros are never significant. Trailing zeros are significant ONLY if a decimal point is present. In calculations, the final result cannot be more precise than the least precise measurement used.

Rules for Counting Sig Figs

  • All non-zero digits → significant (1–9)
  • Zeros between non-zeros → significant (1003 → 4 sf)
  • Leading zeros → NOT significant (0.0023 → 2 sf)
  • Trailing zeros after decimal → significant (2.300 → 4 sf)
  • Trailing zeros without decimal → ambiguous (2300 could be 2, 3 or 4 sf)
  • Scientific notation resolves ambiguity (2.3×10³ = 2 sf)

Arithmetic with Sig Figs

Multiplication/Division: Result has same number of sig figs as the factor with fewest sig figs.

Addition/Subtraction: Result has same number of decimal places as the number with fewest decimal places.

Rounding: If digit to be dropped is 5 followed by non-zero, round up. If exactly 5, round to nearest even.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

When tackling significant figures and rounding practice questions, always apply the weakest link rule. For addition/subtraction, round to the least number of decimal places. For multiplication/division, round to the least number of significant figures. A critical boundary condition: exact numbers (like 2 in 2πr) have infinite significant figures and do not affect the rounding.

Q1 · Sig Figs
The number of significant figures in 0.006720 is:
a) 7
b) 5
c) 4
d) 3
Leading zeros are not significant. 6, 7, 2, and the trailing 0 (since there is a decimal) are significant. Total = 4.
Q2 · Sig Figs
How many significant figures are in 2.000 x 10^4?
a) 1
b) 4
c) 5
d) 8
In scientific notation, all digits in the coefficient are significant. 2.000 has 4 significant figures.
Q3 · Sig Figs
Add 2.34 and 1.2 to the correct number of significant figures.
a) 3.5
b) 3.54
c) 3.6
d) 4.0
In addition, the result must have the same number of decimal places as the number with the fewest decimal places. 1.2 has one decimal place. 2.34 + 1.2 = 3.54 → rounds to 3.5.
Q4 · Sig Figs
Multiply 2.5 by 1.25. The result with correct significant figures is:
a) 3.125
b) 3.13
c) 3.1
d) 3.0
In multiplication, the result must have the same number of sig figs as the factor with the fewest sig figs. 2.5 has 2 sig figs. 2.5 * 1.25 = 3.125 → rounds to 3.1.
Q5 · Sig Figs
Which of the following has 3 significant figures?
a) 0.03
b) 0.0300
c) 300
d) 3.000
0.0300 has 3 sig figs (3, 0, 0). 0.03 has 1. 300 is ambiguous but usually 1. 3.000 has 4.
Error Analysis
Read Detailed Explanation

Error analysis quantifies the uncertainty in physical measurements. Errors can be systematic (consistent, directional) or random (unpredictable, fluctuating). Absolute error is the magnitude of difference between the measured and true value. Relative (or fractional) error is the ratio of absolute error to the true value. When quantities are multiplied or divided, their maximum fractional errors add up.

Absolute error
\( \Delta a = |a_{\text{mean}} - a_i| \)     Mean absolute error: \( \Delta \bar{a} = \frac{\Sigma |\Delta a_i|}{n} \)
Relative error and Percentage error
Relative error: \( \frac{\Delta \bar{a}}{a_{\text{mean}}} \)     % error: \( \left(\frac{\Delta \bar{a}}{a_{\text{mean}}}\right) \times 100\% \)
Error propagation rules (most important for NEET)
\( Z = A \pm B \rightarrow \Delta Z = \Delta A + \Delta B \) (absolute errors add always)
\( Z = AB \) or \( \frac{A}{B} \rightarrow \frac{\Delta Z}{Z} = \frac{\Delta A}{A} + \frac{\Delta B}{B} \) (relative errors add)
\( Z = A^n \rightarrow \frac{\Delta Z}{Z} = n \left(\frac{\Delta A}{A}\right) \) (power amplifies relative error)
In multiplication/division/powers: always add RELATIVE errors. In addition/subtraction: always add ABSOLUTE errors.
NEET Trap — Errors in Powers

If Z = A²B/√C, then ΔZ/Z = 2(ΔA/A) + (ΔB/B) + ½(ΔC/C). The power multiplies the relative error — this is the most common NEET error-analysis question type.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

In error analysis practice, remember that errors always add up to represent the maximum possible uncertainty. Even if a physical formula involves subtraction (e.g., Z = A - B), the absolute errors are added (ΔZ = ΔA + ΔB). For powers (Z = A^n), the relative error is multiplied by the power index: ΔZ/Z = n(ΔA/A).

Q1 · Error
If percentage errors in L and M are 2% and 3%, then the max percentage error in L²M is:
a) 5%
b) 2%
c) 6%
d) 7%
Z = L²M. ΔZ/Z = 2(ΔL/L) + ΔM/M = 2(2%) + 3% = 7%. Power multiplies relative error.
Q2 · Error
The length and breadth of a rectangle are (5.7 ± 0.1) cm and (3.4 ± 0.2) cm. The area with error limits is:
a) (19.38 ± 1.48) cm²
b) (19.38 ± 0.3) cm²
c) (19.38 ± 0.2) cm²
d) (19.38 ± 0.02) cm²
Area A = 5.7 * 3.4 = 19.38. Relative error ΔA/A = ΔL/L + ΔB/B = 0.1/5.7 + 0.2/3.4 = 0.0175 + 0.0588 = 0.0763. ΔA = 19.38 * 0.0763 = 1.48.
Q3 · Error
If x = A³/B², and % error in A is 1% and B is 2%, the % error in x is:
a) 1%
b) -1%
c) 7%
d) 5%
Max % error = 3(%A) + 2(%B) = 3(1%) + 2(2%) = 3% + 4% = 7%. Errors ALWAYS add up, even for division.
Q4 · Error
Two resistances R1 = (100 ± 3) Ω and R2 = (200 ± 4) Ω are connected in series. The equivalent resistance is:
a) (300 ± 1) Ω
b) (300 ± 7) Ω
c) (300 ± 12) Ω
d) (300 ± 5) Ω
In series, Req = R1 + R2 = 300. Absolute errors add up: ΔR = 3 + 4 = 7. Result: (300 ± 7) Ω.
Q5 · Error
A physical quantity is measured and the result is given as (2.00 ± 0.02) m. What is the percentage error?
a) 0.02%
b) 2%
c) 0.01%
d) 1%
% error = (Absolute error / True value) * 100 = (0.02 / 2.00) * 100 = 1%.
Assertion-Reason — Units & Measurement
Read Detailed Explanation

Assertion-Reason questions in Units & Measurement frequently contrast dimensions with units. A classic boundary condition: A quantity can have units but be dimensionless (e.g., plane angle has units of radians but dimension [M^0L^0T^0]). However, a quantity cannot have dimensions if it lacks units. Ensure the Reason correctly explains the fundamental laws governing the Assertion.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: The dimensional analysis cannot determine the value of the constant in the formula T = 2π√(l/g).
Reason: The constant 2π is dimensionless, and dimensional analysis only deals with quantities that have dimensions.
A B C D E
Answer: (A) — Both A and R are true, and R is the correct explanation. Dimensional analysis cannot determine dimensionless constants because it only tracks the powers of M, L, T etc. The value 2π must be found experimentally. R correctly explains why A is true.
AR-2
Assertion: An equation that is dimensionally correct is necessarily a physically correct equation.
Reason: Dimensional analysis checks the consistency of units on both sides of an equation.
A B C D E
Answer: (C) — A is FALSE, R is TRUE. R correctly describes what dimensional analysis does. However, A is wrong: a dimensionally correct equation is NOT necessarily physically correct. Example: KE = m²v³ is dimensionally wrong (ruled out), but KE = (3/16)mv² is dimensionally correct yet physically wrong. Dimensional analysis is a necessary but not sufficient test.
AR-3
Assertion: In the expression eˢⁱⁿ θ, the angle θ must be dimensionless.
Reason: All arguments of mathematical functions (sin, cos, log, exp) must be pure dimensionless numbers.
A B C D E
Answer: (A) — Both A and R are true, and R is the correct explanation. sin(θ) is dimensionless (it's a ratio of lengths). For it to make dimensional sense inside an exponential, θ must itself be dimensionless. This rule — that function arguments must be dimensionless — is a fundamental principle of dimensional consistency.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

A final check for Units & Measurement questions: always verify if physical constants are dimensional or dimensionless. For example, the gravitational constant (G) and Plancks constant (h) have complex dimensional formulas, whereas the refractive index is strictly dimensionless. Memorizing dimensions of key constants drastically speeds up elimination in multiple-choice questions.

Q4 · Dimensional analysis — deriving formula
Using dimensional analysis, which formula for the time period T of a simple pendulum (l = length, g = acceleration) is correct?
a) T = k√(l/g)
b) T = k√(g/l)
c) T = k·l/g
d) T = k·g/l
T ∝ lˣ·gʸ. [T¹] = [L]ˣ[LT⁻²]ʸ = L^(x+y) T^(-2y). Comparing: -2y=1 → y=-½; x+y=0 → x=½. So T = k·l^½·g^(-½) = k√(l/g). The actual constant k=2π (found by experiment, not dimensions). Answer: (a).
2

Motion in a Straight Line (Kinematics 1D)

⭐ NEET Weight: 2–3 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 2 | Displacement, velocity, acceleration, SUVAT equations, free fall, relative velocity

Key Definitions & Distinctions
Read Detailed Explanation

In 1D kinematics, fundamentally distinguish between scalar and vector quantities. Distance is the total path length (scalar, always positive), while displacement is the shortest straight-line distance between initial and final points (vector, can be zero or negative). Similarly, speed is the rate of distance covered, whereas velocity is the rate of change of displacement.

Distance vs Displacement

Distance: Total path length traveled (scalar, always ≥ 0)
Displacement: Change in position vector (vector, can be negative)
Key: Average speed = total distance/time ≥ |average velocity| = |displacement|/time. Equality only when motion is in one direction without reversal.

Speed vs Velocity

Speed: Distance per unit time (scalar, always ≥ 0)
Velocity: Displacement per unit time (vector)
Instantaneous speed: Always equals magnitude of instantaneous velocity.
Average speed: May not equal magnitude of average velocity.

Instantaneous velocity — slope of x-t graph at that point
\( v = \lim_{\Delta t \to 0} \frac{\Delta x}{\Delta t} = \frac{dx}{dt} \)
Instantaneous acceleration a = dv/dt = d²x/dt² = slope of v-t graph at that point
Practice Problems For This Concept
Q1 · Definitions
The numerical ratio of average velocity to average speed is:
a) Always less than one
b) Equal to or less than one
c) Always equal to one
d) Equal to or greater than one
Displacement is always ≤ Distance. Therefore, |Average Velocity| ≤ Average Speed.
Q2 · Definitions
A particle moving in one direction has velocity +20 m/s for 3s, then -10 m/s for 2s. The displacement is:
a) 80 m
b) 60 m
c) 40 m
d) 20 m
Displacement = (+20×3) + (−10×2) = 60 − 20 = 40 m.
Q3 · Definitions
A person travels half the total distance with speed v1 and the remaining half with speed v2. The average speed is:
a) 2v1v2/(v1+v2)
b) (v1+v2)/2
c) √(v1v2)
d) v1v2/(v1+v2)
Total distance = 2d. Total time = d/v1 + d/v2. Average speed = 2d / (d/v1 + d/v2) = 2v1v2/(v1+v2).
Q4 · Definitions
Can a body have constant speed but varying velocity?
a) No
b) Yes, in circular motion
c) Yes, in straight line motion
d) Only if acceleration is zero
In uniform circular motion, speed is constant but direction changes continuously, so velocity varies.
Q5 · Definitions
The slope of the displacement-time graph at any given point gives:
a) Average velocity
b) Average speed
c) Instantaneous velocity
d) Instantaneous acceleration
Slope of x-t graph = dx/dt, which is the instantaneous velocity.
SUVAT Equations — The Core 5 (Uniform Acceleration Only)
Read Detailed Explanation

The five SUVAT equations describe uniformly accelerated linear motion. They fundamentally relate displacement (s), initial velocity (u), final velocity (v), acceleration (a), and time (t). Critical condition: These equations are STRICTLY valid only when acceleration is constant. If acceleration varies with time or position, you must use calculus (integration and differentiation) instead of these algebraic equations.

The 5 kinematic equations (valid only when acceleration is CONSTANT)
① \( v = u + at \)
② \( s = ut + \frac{1}{2}at^2 \)
③ \( v^2 = u^2 + 2as \)
④ \( s = \frac{u+v}{2}t \)     [average velocity × time]
⑤ \( s_n = u + \frac{a(2n-1)}{2} \)     [distance in nth SECOND specifically]
u=initial velocity, v=final velocity, a=acceleration, s=displacement, t=time, n=nth second (integer). Equation ⑤ is extremely NEET-tested.
Graph Interpretations — High NEET Frequency

• Slope of x-t graph = velocity (steep = fast, flat = stationary, negative slope = moving back)
• Slope of v-t graph = acceleration
• Area under v-t graph = displacement (NOT distance — for distance, take |area| for negative sections)
• Area under a-t graph = change in velocity (Δv)
• Parabolic x-t graph → uniform acceleration; straight v-t → uniform acceleration

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Kinematics practice questions often involve identifying whether the motion has constant or variable acceleration. A classic trap is applying SUVAT equations to a variable force scenario (like a spring). Always verify a = constant first. If velocity is given as a function of position, remember to use a = v(dv/dx) instead of standard time derivatives.

Q1 · SUVAT
A body starts from rest with a = 4 m/s². The distance covered in the 5th second is:
a) 16 m
b) 18 m
c) 20 m
d) 36 m
sₙ = u + a(2n−1)/2 = 0 + 4(2(5)−1)/2 = 18 m.
Q2 · SUVAT
A car accelerates from rest at 2 m/s² for 10s. Its final velocity is:
a) 10 m/s
b) 15 m/s
c) 20 m/s
d) 25 m/s
v = u + at = 0 + 2(10) = 20 m/s.
Q3 · SUVAT
A train slows down from 20 m/s to rest in 100 m. Its retardation is:
a) 2 m/s²
b) 4 m/s²
c) 1 m/s²
d) 0.5 m/s²
v² = u² + 2as. 0 = 20² + 2a(100) → 400 = -200a → a = -2 m/s². Retardation is 2.
Q4 · SUVAT
A particle starts from rest and moves with uniform acceleration. The distance covered in first 2 seconds to that in next 2 seconds is in ratio:
a) 1:1
b) 1:2
c) 1:4
d) 1:3
Distance in first 2s = 1/2 a(2)² = 2a. Distance in 4s = 1/2 a(4)² = 8a. Distance in next 2s = 8a - 2a = 6a. Ratio = 2a:6a = 1:3.
Q5 · SUVAT
The area under a velocity-time graph gives:
a) Acceleration
b) Displacement
c) Instantaneous velocity
d) Force
The integral of v dt is displacement, which corresponds to the area under the v-t graph.
Free Fall — Special Case (a = g downward)
Read Detailed Explanation

Free fall is a classic case of 1D uniformly accelerated motion where gravity is the sole force acting on a body. The acceleration is constant (a = -g ≈ -9.8 m/s²) directed downwards. Air resistance is typically neglected. A vital boundary condition: at the maximum height of a vertically thrown object, instantaneous velocity is exactly zero, but acceleration remains -g.

Key Free-Fall Results

• g = 9.8 m/s² ≈ 10 m/s² (take as 10 in NEET unless stated)
• Time of ascent = Time of descent (same height)
• Speed at ground = speed with which it was thrown (same height)
• At highest point: v = 0, but a = g ≠ 0

Thrown Upward from Ground

• Max height H = u²/2g
• Time to reach top: t = u/g
• Total time of flight: T = 2u/g
• Velocity at height h: v = √(u²−2gh)

Dropped from Rest (u=0)

• v = gt
• h = ½gt²
• v = √(2gh)
• Distance in nth second: sₙ = g(2n−1)/2
• Ratio of distances: 1:3:5:7... (odd numbers)

Practice Problems For This Concept
Q1 · Free Fall
At maximum height, for a ball thrown upward:
a) Velocity and acceleration are zero
b) Velocity is zero, acceleration = g
c) Both are non-zero
d) Velocity is non-zero, a = 0
Momentarily at rest v=0, but gravity always pulls at g=9.8 m/s².
Q2 · Free Fall
A stone is dropped from rest. Ratio of distances in 1st, 2nd, 3rd seconds is:
a) 1:2:3
b) 1:4:9
c) 1:2:4
d) 1:3:5
Galileo's law of odd numbers. sₙ ∝ (2n-1). Ratio = 1:3:5.
Q3 · Free Fall
A body thrown vertically up takes time t1 to reach max height. Time taken to fall back is t2. If air resistance is neglected:
a) t1 = t2
b) t1 > t2
c) t1 < t2
d) Cannot be determined
Without air resistance, time of ascent = time of descent = u/g.
Q4 · Free Fall
Two bodies of masses 1kg and 5kg are dropped from the same height. The ratio of their velocities upon hitting the ground is:
a) 1:5
b) 5:1
c) 1:1
d) 1:25
v = √(2gh). It is independent of mass. Ratio is 1:1.
Q5 · Free Fall
A ball is dropped from a tower of height 20m. Find its velocity just before hitting the ground (g=10).
a) 10 m/s
b) 20 m/s
c) 30 m/s
d) 40 m/s
v = √(2gh) = √(2 * 10 * 20) = √400 = 20 m/s.
Relative Velocity
Read Detailed Explanation

Relative velocity describes the velocity of one object as observed from another moving frame of reference. Fundamentally, the velocity of object A relative to object B is v_AB = v_A - v_B. In 1D motion, this translates to subtracting speeds for objects moving in the same direction, and adding speeds for objects moving in opposite directions.

Relative velocity of A with respect to B
\( v_{AB} = v_A - v_B \)
Same direction: v_rel = v_A − v_B (smaller number). Opposite directions: v_rel = v_A + v_B (larger number).

Rain-Man Problem

If rain falls vertically at v_r and man walks at v_m, rain appears to come from front at angle θ = tan⁻¹(v_m/v_r). Man must tilt umbrella forward at this angle. Relative velocity of rain w.r.t. man = v_rain − v_man (vector subtraction).

Train Problems

Two trains passing each other: time = (L₁+L₂)/(v₁+v₂) [opposite] or (L₁+L₂)/(v₁−v₂) [same direction]. Train crossing a pole: time = L/v. Train crossing a bridge: time = (L+d)/v where d = bridge length.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

For relative velocity practice questions, establishing a strict sign convention is crucial. Choose one direction (e.g., right or upwards) as positive and stick to it for all vectors. When an observer is on a moving platform (like a train), the relative velocity formula allows you to treat the platform as stationary, dramatically simplifying the mathematical setup.

Q1 · Relative
Trains A (60 km/h) and B (90 km/h) move toward each other. Relative speed is:
a) 150 km/h
b) 30 km/h
c) 75 km/h
d) 45 km/h
Opposite directions = v_A + v_B = 150 km/h.
Q2 · Relative
A boat moves with 10 km/h in still water. River flows at 5 km/h. Time to go 15 km downstream is:
a) 3 h
b) 2 h
c) 1 h
d) 1.5 h
Downstream speed = 10 + 5 = 15 km/h. Time = Distance/Speed = 15/15 = 1 h.
Q3 · Relative
Rain is falling vertically at 4 m/s. A man walks horizontally at 3 m/s. At what angle to the vertical should he hold his umbrella?
a) tan⁻¹(4/3)
b) tan⁻¹(3/4)
c) 30°
d) 45°
tan θ = v_man / v_rain = 3/4. θ = tan⁻¹(3/4).
Q4 · Relative
Two trains of lengths 100m and 150m are moving in the same direction at 50 km/h and 32 km/h. Time taken to cross each other is:
a) 50 s
b) 10 s
c) 25 s
d) 100 s
Relative speed = 50 - 32 = 18 km/h = 18 * (5/18) = 5 m/s. Total distance = 100+150=250m. Time = 250/5 = 50s.
Q5 · Relative
A monkey climbs a 10m rope with 2 m/s relative to rope. Rope is pulled up at 1 m/s. Velocity of monkey wrt ground is:
a) 1 m/s
b) 2 m/s
c) 0.5 m/s
d) 3 m/s
v_mg = v_mr + v_rg = 2 + 1 = 3 m/s.
Assertion-Reason — Kinematics 1D
Read Detailed Explanation

Assertion-Reason questions in 1D Kinematics often test the relationship between velocity and acceleration vectors. A critical principle: an object can have zero velocity but non-zero acceleration (e.g., at the peak of a vertical throw). Also, if velocity and acceleration have opposite signs, the object is slowing down (retardation), regardless of whether velocity is positive or negative.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: Average speed of a body over an interval of time is always greater than or equal to the magnitude of average velocity.
Reason: Total distance traveled by a body is always greater than or equal to the magnitude of total displacement.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. Speed = distance/time ≥ |displacement|/time = |velocity| because distance ≥ |displacement| always. Equality holds when particle moves in a straight line in one direction only (no reversal).
AR-2
Assertion: A particle can have zero velocity and still have a non-zero acceleration.
Reason: Velocity and acceleration are independent physical quantities defined at the same instant of time.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R is the correct explanation. At the topmost point of vertical projection: v=0 but a=g. More precisely, a=dv/dt — it's the instantaneous rate of change of velocity. Even when v=0, v can be changing (a≠0). The reason correctly explains the underlying physics.
AR-3
Assertion: The slope of the displacement-time graph gives the instantaneous speed of the particle.
Reason: Speed is the magnitude of velocity and velocity is the rate of change of displacement.
ABCDE
Answer: (D) — A is FALSE, R is TRUE. The slope of x-t graph gives instantaneous VELOCITY (not speed). Velocity can be negative (when particle moves in negative direction), which the x-t slope captures. Speed is always positive (= |slope|). The reason correctly defines speed and velocity, making R true. But A says "speed" instead of "velocity" — hence A is false.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

When interpreting kinematics graphs, remember that the slope of a position-time graph gives velocity, and the slope of a velocity-time graph gives acceleration. The area under a velocity-time graph represents displacement, while the area under an acceleration-time graph gives the change in velocity. Non-linear curves indicate variable rates, demanding calculus for precise area or slope determination.

Q3 · Highest point trap
A ball thrown vertically upward reaches its maximum height. At the maximum height, which statement is correct?
a) Both velocity and acceleration are zero
b) Velocity is zero but acceleration = g downward
c) Both are non-zero
d) Velocity is non-zero but acceleration is zero
At maximum height, v = 0 (momentarily at rest). But gravity never stops — it always accelerates objects downward at g = 9.8 m/s². So a = g downward even though v = 0. This is the most common NEET trap in kinematics: zero velocity does NOT mean zero acceleration.
3

Motion in a Plane (Kinematics 2D)

⭐ NEET Weight: 2–3 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 3 | Vectors, projectile motion, uniform circular motion

Vector Algebra — Complete Rules
Read Detailed Explanation

Vector algebra extends mathematics to quantities with direction. Key principles include the Triangle and Parallelogram laws for vector addition. The dot product yields a scalar, representing the projection of one vector onto another, becoming zero for perpendicular vectors. The cross product yields a vector perpendicular to both, governed by the right-hand rule, and becomes zero for parallel vectors.

Vector Addition Methods

Triangle law: Place vectors head-to-tail; resultant = closing side from tail of first to head of last.
Parallelogram law: Two vectors from same point; resultant = diagonal of parallelogram.
Resultant magnitude: R = √(A²+B²+2AB cosθ) where θ is angle between A and B.
Direction: tan α = B sinθ/(A + B cosθ)

Dot Product & Cross Product

Dot product (scalar): A·B = AB cosθ. A·A = A². î·î = ĵ·ĵ = k̂·k̂ = 1. î·ĵ = 0.
Work = F·d = Fd cosθ

Cross product (vector): |A×B| = AB sinθ. î×ĵ = k̂, ĵ×k̂ = î, k̂×î = ĵ (cyclic).
Torque τ = r×F. Angular momentum L = r×p

Unit vector, resolution into components
\( \hat{a} = \frac{\vec{A}}{|\vec{A}|} \)     \( \vec{A} = A_x\hat{i} + A_y\hat{j} + A_z\hat{k} \)
\( A_x = A \cos \theta, A_y = A \sin \theta \)     \( |\vec{A}| = \sqrt{A_x^2 + A_y^2 + A_z^2} \)
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

In 2D kinematics practice, always resolve vectors into orthogonal components (usually x and y axes). This conceptually splits 2D motion into two independent 1D motions that share only the parameter of time. Remember that orthogonal vectors do not affect each other; a horizontal force cannot alter the vertical component of velocity.

a) Mechanical Energy
b) \text{Total } Energy
c) Momentum
d) Depends on the specific system
Conservation laws in vector algebra — complete rules strictly depend on whether the system is isolated, conservative, or non-conservative.

a) A parabola
b) A straight line with positive slope
c) A straight line parallel to the time axis
d) A hyperbola
Steady state implies constancy over time. Thus, the graph of the parameter vs time is a horizontal line.

c) It is a fundamental property

c) Environmental factors (e.g. friction/air resistance)
d) All of the above

a) Analyzing macroscopic systems
b) Determining microscopic properties
c) Both A and B
d) None of the above
The principles of vector algebra — complete rules span across multiple domains of physics, affecting both large scale and quantum scale phenomena.
Projectile Motion — All Cases
Read Detailed Explanation

Projectile motion is two-dimensional motion under constant vertical gravitational acceleration, with zero horizontal acceleration (ignoring air resistance). The trajectory is always a parabola. Critical boundary conditions: Horizontal velocity remains constant throughout the flight. At the highest point, vertical velocity is zero, meaning the overall velocity vector is purely horizontal and strictly perpendicular to the acceleration vector.

Projectile launched at angle θ with horizontal, initial speed u
Horizontal: \( x = (u \cos \theta) t \) (uniform; no acceleration)
Vertical: \( y = (u \sin \theta) t - \frac{1}{2}gt^2 \) (uniform acceleration g downward)
Time of flight: \( T = \frac{2u \sin \theta}{g} \)
Maximum height: \( H = \frac{u^2 \sin^2\theta}{2g} \)
Horizontal range: \( R = \frac{u^2 \sin 2\theta}{g} \)
Maximum range: \( R_{\text{max}} = \frac{u^2}{g} \)     (at \( \theta = 45^\circ \))
Equation of path: \( y = x \tan \theta - \frac{gx^2}{2u^2 \cos^2\theta} \)     [parabola]
R is same for complementary angles: θ and (90°−θ) give same range. At angle 45°, range is maximum. At top of trajectory: vₓ = u cosθ, vᵧ = 0, speed = u cosθ

Horizontal Projection (θ = 0°)

Launched horizontally from height H with speed u.
• Time to hit ground: t = √(2H/g)
• Range: R = u√(2H/g)
• Velocity at ground: vₓ = u, vᵧ = gt
• Speed at ground: √(u²+v_y²)
• Angle with horizontal: tan α = vᵧ/u

Key Results to Memorise

• θ and (90°−θ): same range, different time/height
• Maximum range at θ = 45°: Rₘₐₓ = u²/g
• H/R = tanθ/4
• At max height, KE = ½m(u cosθ)²
• Angle of velocity with horizontal at any t: tan φ = vᵧ/vₓ
• Projectile path is parabola

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

For projectile motion questions, key formulas for Maximum Height, Time of Flight, and Range are derived assuming launch and landing occur at the same horizontal level. If a projectile is launched from a cliff, do not blindly use the standard Range formula. Instead, rely on the core principle: isolate the vertical motion to find time, then use horizontal motion to find distance.

d) A hyperbola
Steady state implies constancy over time. Thus, the graph of the parameter vs time is a horizontal line.

Uniform Circular Motion
Read Detailed Explanation

Uniform Circular Motion involves an object moving in a circle with constant speed. Although speed is constant, the direction of velocity continuously changes, meaning the object is always accelerating. This is governed by centripetal acceleration (v²/r), which strictly points toward the center. Critical principle: The work done by the centripetal force is always zero because it is perpendicular to displacement.

Circular motion quantities
\( \omega = \frac{2\pi}{T} = 2\pi f \)     \( v = r\omega \)
Centripetal acceleration: \( a_c = \frac{v^2}{r} = r\omega^2 \) (directed toward center)
Centripetal force: \( F_c = \frac{mv^2}{r} = mr\omega^2 \) (always toward center)
Period: \( T = \frac{2\pi r}{v} = \frac{2\pi}{\omega} \)
Centripetal force is not a new force — it's the NAME given to whatever force provides inward acceleration (tension, gravity, friction, normal force etc.)
Common Mistake — "Centrifugal Force"

Centrifugal force is a pseudo-force that exists only in the rotating (non-inertial) reference frame. In the inertial (ground) frame, there is NO centrifugal force — only centripetal (inward). NEET questions are about the inertial frame unless specified. Never include centrifugal force in a free-body diagram in the ground frame.

Car on Circular Road (Flat)

Friction provides centripetal force.
Max speed: v_max = √(μₛRg) = √(μₛrg)
If v > v_max → car skids outward.

Banked Road

Tan θ = v²/rg (frictionless ideal banking)
Safe speed range with friction: v between v_min and v_max.
Banking allows higher speeds without relying on friction.

Vertical Circle

Min speed at top: v_top = √(rg)
Min speed at bottom: v_bottom = √(5rg)
T_bottom − T_top = 6mg
(Energy conservation used throughout)

Assertion-Reason — Motion in a Plane
Read Detailed Explanation

Assertion-Reason statements for 2D motion often test circular motion and projectiles. A common conceptual trap: in uniform circular motion, the acceleration vector is NOT constant because its direction constantly shifts toward the center. Thus, SUVAT equations cannot be used for the vector form of circular motion, though angular equations (ω, α) can be used if angular acceleration is constant.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1 · Projectile
Assertion: The horizontal range of a projectile is the same for angles 30° and 60°.
Reason: sin 2θ = sin 2(90°−θ), so complementary angles give equal ranges.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. R = u²sin2θ/g. For θ=30°: sin60°=√3/2. For θ=60°: sin120°=√3/2. Same! R correctly states that sin2(90°−θ) = sin(180°−2θ) = sin2θ, proving complementary angles give equal range. The 60° projectile goes higher but takes longer; the 30° one goes flatter but same horizontal range.
AR-2 · Circular Motion
Assertion: In uniform circular motion, the acceleration of the particle is always perpendicular to its velocity.
Reason: In uniform circular motion, the speed remains constant but direction changes continuously.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. When speed is constant but direction changes, the only acceleration is centripetal (center-directed) which is always perpendicular to the velocity (tangential). R correctly explains why A is true — since no speed change occurs, there's no tangential acceleration, only centripetal (perpendicular) acceleration.
AR-3 · Projectile Acceleration
Assertion: A projectile thrown at 45° has zero acceleration at the highest point.
Reason: At the highest point of projectile motion, the vertical velocity component is zero.
ABCDE
Answer: (D) — A is FALSE, R is TRUE. Throughout the entire projectile flight, the acceleration is always g downward (gravity never stops). At the highest point, vᵧ = 0 (R is true), but acceleration = g = 9.8 m/s² downward always. The assertion incorrectly says acceleration is zero — it confuses zero velocity component with zero acceleration.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

When solving relative motion in 2D (like river-boat or rain-man problems), draw vector triangles. The absolute velocity of the boat is the vector sum of its velocity relative to water and the water's velocity. For shortest path, point upstream; for shortest time, point directly across. Time to cross depends exclusively on the velocity component perpendicular to the flow.

Q2 · Projectile at top of trajectory
A projectile is fired at 60° with 40 m/s. At the highest point, its speed is:
a) 0 m/s
b) 40 m/s
c) 20 m/s
d) 20√3 m/s
At the highest point, vertical component = 0. Only horizontal component remains: vₓ = u cosθ = 40×cos60° = 40×0.5 = 20 m/s. Speed is NEVER zero for θ ≠ 90° — horizontal velocity is always retained. This is a classic NEET trap.
Q3 · Centripetal acceleration
A particle moves in a circle of radius 0.5 m at 2 m/s. Its centripetal acceleration is:
a) 1 m/s²
b) 2 m/s²
c) 0.25 m/s²
d) 8 m/s²
aₐ = v²/r = (2)²/0.5 = 4/0.5 = 8 m/s². The centripetal acceleration is always directed toward the center of the circle. At constant speed, the acceleration only changes direction, not magnitude — this is why kinetic energy doesn't change in uniform circular motion.
4

Laws of Motion

⭐ NEET Weight: 3–4 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 4 | Newton's laws, friction, circular motion, impulse, momentum

Newton's Three Laws — Crystal Clear
Read Detailed Explanation

Newton's First Law defines inertia: an object remains at rest or in uniform motion unless acted upon by a net external force. The Second Law provides the fundamental equation F_net = dp/dt (force equals rate of change of momentum), simplifying to F = ma for constant mass. The Third Law dictates that action and reaction forces are equal, opposite, and act on DIFFERENT bodies.

First Law — Law of Inertia

Every body continues to be in its state of rest or uniform motion in a straight line unless compelled by an external net force. Inertia = resistance to change in state of motion. Inertia ∝ mass. If F_net = 0, then a = 0 (either at rest OR moving at constant velocity).

Second Law — F = ma

F_net = dp/dt = ma (constant mass). The net force determines acceleration — not position or velocity. F is a vector equation; direction of net force = direction of acceleration. F at an instant determines a at that instant only (local law). Unit: 1 N = 1 kg·m·s⁻².

Third Law — Action-Reaction

For every action, there is an equal and opposite reaction. F_AB = −F_BA. Key points:
• Acts on DIFFERENT bodies (so they don't cancel)
• Both forces are simultaneous
• Neither causes the other — they're mutual
• Internal forces within a system sum to zero

Impulse-Momentum theorem
Impulse: \( J = F \cdot \Delta t = \Delta p = m(v - u) \)
Impulse = change in momentum. Used when force acts for very short time (batting a ball, collision). Unit: N·s = kg·m/s
Conservation of Linear Momentum
If \( F_{\text{ext}} = 0 \), then \( p_{\text{total}} = \text{constant} \rightarrow m_1u_1 + m_2u_2 = m_1v_1 + m_2v_2 \)
Applies during collisions (external forces negligible during very short collision time). The total momentum of an isolated system is always conserved.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

A fundamental rule for Laws of Motion problems is identifying the system. Once a system is defined, internal forces strictly cancel out due to Newton's Third Law, and only external forces affect the system's overall acceleration. This drastically simplifies complex multi-body problems: treat them as a single mass if they share the same acceleration.

Free Body Diagram — The Method
Read Detailed Explanation

A Free Body Diagram (FBD) visually isolates a single object to analyze all external forces acting exclusively upon it. Critical steps: represent the object as a point mass, draw all force vectors (gravity, normal, tension, friction) originating from the center, and establish a coordinate system. Crucially, never include forces that the object exerts on its surroundings.

Step-by-Step FBD Method for Any NEET Problem

  • Step 1: Identify the system (one body or multiple)
  • Step 2: Draw FBD of each body — show ALL forces: weight (mg↓), normal force (N⊥surface), tension (T along string), friction (f opposing relative motion), applied force (F)
  • Step 3: Choose convenient coordinate axes (usually along and perpendicular to motion/surface)
  • Step 4: Apply ΣF = ma along each axis separately
  • Step 5: Solve the simultaneous equations

Friction — Complete Analysis
Read Detailed Explanation

Friction is an electromagnetic contact force opposing relative motion or the tendency of such motion. Static friction is self-adjusting up to a maximum limit (f_s ≤ μ_s N). Once relative sliding begins, kinetic friction takes over, which is constant and slightly lower than maximum static friction (f_k = μ_k N). Boundary condition: friction is completely independent of the macroscopic area of contact.

Types of friction
Static friction: \( f_s \le \mu_s N \)     (self-adjusting up to max \( = \mu_s N \))
Kinetic friction: \( f_k = \mu_k N \) (constant once sliding begins)
Always: \( \mu_k < \mu_s \)     (kinetic < static friction coefficient)
Friction is independent of: area of contact, velocity (kinetic friction). Friction depends on: normal force N, nature of surfaces (μ). Friction does NOT depend on mass directly — only through N = mg.
SituationNormal Force NFriction fAcceleration a
Block on flat surface, applied force Fmgμₖmg (if sliding)(F − μₖmg)/m
Block on incline angle θ, just slidingmg cosθμₛmg cosθ0 (just begins)
Block sliding down incline angle θmg cosθμₖmg cosθ (up the incline)g(sinθ − μₖcosθ)
Block on incline, pushed upmg cosθμₖmg cosθ (down the incline)g(sinθ + μₖcosθ) deceleration
Lift accelerating upward at am(g+a)μm(g+a)Same as usual but N increased
Lift accelerating downward at am(g−a)μm(g−a)N reduced; weightlessness if a=g
Angle of Friction & Angle of Repose

Angle of friction \lambda : tan \lambda = μₛ (angle that resultant contact force makes with normal when on verge of sliding)
Angle of repose θᵣ: Maximum angle of incline at which block stays stationary. tan θᵣ = μₛ. At angle > θᵣ, block slides. θᵣ = \lambda (angle of friction = angle of repose!)

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

In friction practice questions, never blindly assume static friction equals μ_s N. That is only the limiting (maximum) value. First, calculate the applied force attempting to cause motion. If it is less than the limiting friction, the static friction perfectly balances the applied force. Only use μ_k N when it is explicitly confirmed that the object is sliding.

Connected Bodies — Atwood Machine & Pulley Systems
Read Detailed Explanation

For connected bodies like pulleys or Atwood machines, the core principle is constraint relation: if the string is inextensible, all connected masses share the same magnitude of acceleration. The tension is uniform throughout a massless, frictionless string. For calculations, write individual Newton's Second Law equations for each mass and solve them simultaneously to find acceleration and tension.

Atwood Machine (2 masses over pulley)

m₁ and m₂ (m₁ > m₂) over frictionless pulley.
Acceleration: a = (m₁−m₂)g/(m₁+m₂)
Tension: T = 2m₁m₂g/(m₁+m₂)
When m₁ = m₂: a = 0, T = m₁g (static equilibrium)

Block on Table, Hanging Block

Mass M on table (friction μ), mass m hanging.
a = (mg − μMg)/(M+m)
T = Mg(m + μm)/(M+m) ... but use full FBD approach for confidence.
Block slides only if mg > μMg

Assertion-Reason — Laws of Motion
Read Detailed Explanation

Assertion-Reason questions often test Newton's Third Law. A classic pitfall is believing action and reaction can cancel each other out to yield equilibrium. They cannot, because they act on two completely separate bodies. Another key concept: pseudo forces are not real; they are merely mathematical corrections applied when observing motion from an accelerating (non-inertial) frame of reference.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1 · Newton's 3rd Law
Assertion: Action and reaction forces, being equal and opposite, always cancel each other.
Reason: Action and reaction forces act on different bodies, so they cannot cancel each other.
ABCDE
Answer: (D) — A is FALSE, R is TRUE. Two forces can cancel only when they act on the SAME body. Action-reaction forces act on DIFFERENT bodies — force on A by B, and force on B by A. They can never cancel each other. R correctly explains why A is false. This is one of the most fundamental misunderstandings in Newton's 3rd law.
AR-2 · Friction
Assertion: Kinetic friction is always less than maximum static friction.
Reason: The coefficient of kinetic friction (μₖ) is always less than the coefficient of static friction (μₛ).
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. μₖ < μₛ is experimentally established (sliding surfaces have less interlocking than stationary surfaces). Since fₖ = μₖN and (fₛ)max = μₛN, and μₖ < μₛ, it follows that fₖ < (fₛ)max. Physically: it takes more force to START sliding than to MAINTAIN sliding. This is why a car skids more easily after brakes lock (kinetic) than before (static).
AR-3 · Inertia
Assertion: A passenger in a bus feels pushed backward when the bus accelerates forward.
Reason: The inertia of the passenger tends to keep him at rest when the bus accelerates.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. In the ground (inertial) frame: bus accelerates forward, passenger tends to stay where he was (inertia). So relative to the bus, passenger moves backward. The "push backward" is not a real force but an apparent effect of inertia. R correctly identifies inertia as the reason. This is Newton's First Law in action.
5

Work, Energy & Power

⭐ NEET Weight: 3–4 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 5 | Work, kinetic energy, work-energy theorem, conservative forces, power, collisions

Work — All Cases
Read Detailed Explanation

Work is the dot product of force and displacement (W = F · d), representing energy transfer. Work is zero if force and displacement are perpendicular (e.g., centripetal force). It is positive if the force assists motion, and negative if it opposes it (e.g., kinetic friction). For a variable force, work is the integral of force with respect to position, or the area under an F-x graph.

Work done by a force F through displacement d
\( W = \vec{F} \cdot \vec{d} = Fd \cos \theta \)     (\( \theta = \) angle between \( \vec{F} \) and \( \vec{d} \))
θ=0°: W=Fd (max, positive) | θ=90°: W=0 (no work) | θ=180°: W=−Fd (negative work, opposing motion) | Variable force: W = ∫F·dx = area under F-x graph

Positive Work

Force has component along displacement. Force helps motion. KE increases. Example: gravity on falling ball, tension pulling a block forward.

Negative Work

Force has component against displacement. Force opposes motion. KE decreases. Example: friction (always negative), gravity on rising ball, tension in vertical circle at bottom.

Zero Work

Force ⊥ displacement (θ=90°). No KE change. Example: Normal force on horizontal surface, centripetal force in circular motion, magnetic force on moving charge.

Work-Energy Theorem & Energy Types
Read Detailed Explanation

The Work-Energy Theorem is a universal principle: the net work done by ALL forces (conservative, non-conservative, and pseudo) strictly equals the change in kinetic energy of the system. Mechanical energy involves Kinetic (motion) and Potential (configuration). Potential energy is defined strictly for conservative forces (like gravity or springs) and is independent of the path taken.

Work-Energy Theorem (MOST important theorem in mechanics)
\( W_{\text{net}} = \Delta KE = \frac{1}{2}mv^2 - \frac{1}{2}mu^2 \)
Net work done by ALL forces = change in kinetic energy. True for variable forces too. Bypasses need to find acceleration when only initial and final conditions are known.
Potential energy & Conservation of mechanical energy
Gravitational PE: \( U = mgh \) (reference at ground, \( h \) measured upward)
Spring PE: \( U = \frac{1}{2}kx^2 \)     (\( x = \) compression or extension from natural length)
Conservation: \( KE + PE = \text{constant} \) (only for conservative forces; no friction)
Conservative forces: work depends only on endpoints, not path (gravity, spring, electrostatic). Non-conservative: friction, air resistance — dissipate energy as heat.

Power
Read Detailed Explanation

Power is the time rate at which work is done or energy is transferred (P = dW/dt). In mechanics, instantaneous power is the dot product of force and velocity (P = F · v). A vital boundary condition: an engine delivering constant power will not provide constant force; as the object speeds up, the force exerted must decrease to maintain the constant power output.

Power = rate of doing work
\( P = \frac{W}{t} = \vec{F} \cdot \vec{v} = Fv \cos \theta \)
Unit: Watt (W) = J/s = kg·m²·s⁻³. 1 HP = 746 W. Instantaneous power P = F·v (vector dot product). For constant force and speed, P = Fv.

Collisions — Complete Analysis
Read Detailed Explanation

Collisions involve strong interactive forces over a brief time, conserving total linear momentum for the isolated system. In elastic collisions, total kinetic energy is perfectly conserved. In inelastic collisions, some kinetic energy is lost (often as heat/sound), but momentum remains conserved. A perfectly inelastic collision is the boundary case where bodies stick together, resulting in maximum possible kinetic energy loss.

TypeMomentumKEe (coefficient of restitution)Example
ElasticConserved ✓Conserved ✓e = 1Billiard balls, atomic collisions
Perfectly InelasticConserved ✓NOT conserved ✗e = 0Clay hitting wall; bullet embedding in block
InelasticConserved ✓NOT conserved ✗0 < e < 1Most real collisions
Elastic collision — 1D between m₁ (moving) and m₂ (at rest)
\( v_1 = u_1 \left( \frac{m_1 - m_2}{m_1 + m_2} \right) \)
\( v_2 = u_1 \left( \frac{2m_1}{m_1 + m_2} \right) \)
Special cases: m₁=m₂ → v₁=0, v₂=u₁ (complete transfer of velocity — Newton's cradle!). m₁≫m₂ → v₁≈u₁, v₂≈2u₁. m₁≪m₂ → v₁≈−u₁ (bounces back), v₂≈0.
Perfectly inelastic collision — both stick together
\( v_{\text{common}} = \frac{m_1u_1 + m_2u_2}{m_1 + m_2} \) [momentum conservation only]
Loss in KE: \( \Delta KE = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{m_1m_2}{m_1 + m_2} \right) (u_1 - u_2)^2 \)
Coefficient of Restitution e (NEET Advanced)

e = (relative speed of separation)/(relative speed of approach) = (v₂−v₁)/(u₁−u₂). For ball bouncing from floor: e = √(h'/h) where h = drop height, h' = bounce height. e² = h'/h.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

In Work, Energy & Power questions, default to energy conservation rather than kinematics if the path is complex (like a roller coaster or pendulum) and time is not required. Non-conservative forces like friction dissipate mechanical energy. If friction is present, use the modified theorem: Initial Mechanical Energy + Work by Non-Conservative Forces = Final Mechanical Energy.

Assertion-Reason — Work, Energy & Power
Read Detailed Explanation

Assertion-Reason questions frequently probe conservative forces. A fundamental test: if the work done in a closed loop is exactly zero, the force is conservative. Also, potential energy is fundamentally a relative concept; we define a zero reference point (like the ground or infinity). However, the CHANGE in potential energy between two points is an absolute, physically meaningful quantity.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1 · Conservative force
Assertion: Work done by friction on a closed path is not zero.
Reason: Friction is a non-conservative force.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. A non-conservative force is defined as one for which work done on a closed path is NOT zero. Friction always opposes motion, so on any path (including a round trip), it does negative work — the total is never zero. R correctly defines why A is true: that's the very definition of non-conservative.
AR-2 · Momentum in collision
Assertion: In an inelastic collision, linear momentum is conserved but kinetic energy is not.
Reason: During collision, internal forces between colliding objects violate Newton's third law.
ABCDE
Answer: (C) — A is TRUE, R is FALSE. Newton's 3rd law is NEVER violated — internal forces between objects always form action-reaction pairs. Momentum is conserved because there's no external force during the brief collision. KE is not conserved because some energy converts to heat, sound, and deformation. The reason is completely wrong — internal forces follow Newton's 3rd law perfectly.
AR-3 · Zero work by centripetal force
Assertion: The speed of a particle in uniform circular motion is constant, even though a centripetal force acts on it.
Reason: Centripetal force is always perpendicular to velocity, so it does zero work on the particle.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. By work-energy theorem: ΔKE = W_net. If centripetal force does zero work (because it's ⊥ to velocity), then ΔKE = 0, so KE = ½mv² = constant → speed = constant. R is the precise physical reason why speed doesn't change in uniform circular motion despite a force acting.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

When tackling collision problems, always apply conservation of momentum as it holds for all types of collisions in isolated systems. Use the coefficient of restitution (e)—the ratio of relative velocity of separation to relative velocity of approach—to handle kinetic energy states. Remember that e=1 for perfectly elastic and e=0 for perfectly inelastic collisions.

Q1 · Work-energy theorem
A body of mass 2 kg initially at rest is acted on by F = 4t N. Work done in first 2 seconds is:
a) 16 J
b) 64/3 J
c) 8 J
d) 32 J
a = F/m = 4t/2 = 2t. v = ∫a dt = t². At t=2s: v=4 m/s. W = ΔKE = ½×2×(4²)−0 = ½×2×16 = 16 J. Wait — let me recalculate using work integral: W = ∫F·v dt = ∫(4t)(t²)dt from 0 to 2 = ∫4t³dt = [t⁴]₀² = 16 J. But by WE theorem: W=ΔKE = ½mv² = ½(2)(4²) = 16 J. Actually answer is 16 J (a). Note: v = ∫a dt = ∫2t dt = t²; at t=2, v=4 m/s. W = ½(2)(16) = 16 J.
Q2 · Spring PE
A spring of spring constant 200 N/m is compressed by 10 cm. Energy stored is:
a) 2 J
b) 0.2 J
c) 1 J
d) 20 J
U = ½kx² = ½×200×(0.10)² = ½×200×0.01 = 1 J. Always convert cm to m first! x = 10 cm = 0.1 m. This is the most common error — students forget to convert units and get 200 J instead.
Q4 · Zero work
A porter carries a load on his head and walks on a horizontal road for 10 m. The work done by the normal force (from load) on his head is:
a) Zero
b) Positive
c) Negative
d) mgd
The load pushes DOWN on the head (force = mg downward). Displacement is HORIZONTAL. Angle between force and displacement = 90°. W = Fd cos90° = 0. Zero work! Similarly, the porter's head exerts upward force on load (by Newton's 3rd law), also perpendicular to horizontal displacement → zero work. This is why walking on flat ground doesn't tire you out mechanically (in ideal case).
Q5 · Power
A car engine develops 45 kW at 90 km/h. Retarding force on car is:
a) 500 N
b) 1000 N
c) 1800 N
d) 2000 N
At constant velocity, driving force = retarding force. v = 90 km/h = 25 m/s. P = F×v → F = P/v = 45000/25 = 1800 N. The engine force equals retarding force at constant speed (net force = 0, acceleration = 0 by Newton's 1st law).
6

System of Particles & Rotational Motion

⭐ NEET Weight: 3–5 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 6 | Centre of mass, angular momentum, moment of inertia, rolling motion, torque

Centre of Mass
Read Detailed Explanation

The Centre of Mass (COM) is a unique theoretical point where the entire mass of a system can be assumed to be concentrated for translational motion analysis. Crucial principle: internal forces cannot alter the velocity of the COM. The COM only accelerates if a net external force is applied. If external forces sum to zero, the COM moves with a constant velocity.

Position of centre of mass
\( x_{\text{cm}} = \frac{m_1x_1 + m_2x_2 + \dots}{m_1 + m_2 + \dots} = \frac{\Sigma m_ix_i}{M} \)
Similarly for \( y_{\text{cm}} \) and \( z_{\text{cm}} \)
CM of uniform rod: midpoint. CM of uniform ring: centre. CM of uniform disk: centre. CM of hemisphere (solid) of radius R: 3R/8 from flat face. CM of semicircular ring: 2R/π from centre.
CM of Uniform Bodies — Memorise for NEET

• Solid hemisphere: h = 3R/8 from base
• Hollow hemisphere: h = R/2 from base
• Solid cone: h = H/4 from base (H = height of cone)
• Hollow cone: h = H/3 from base
• Semicircular ring: 2R/π from centre
• Semicircular disk: 4R/3π from centre

Torque, Angular Momentum & Newton's Rotational Laws
Read Detailed Explanation

Torque (τ = r × F) is the rotational analogue of force, causing angular acceleration. Angular momentum (L = r × p) is the rotational equivalent of linear momentum. Newton's Second Law for rotation dictates τ_net = dL/dt. If net external torque is zero, total angular momentum is strictly conserved—a foundational law underlying planetary motion and spinning ice skaters.

Torque (rotational analog of force)
\( \vec{\tau} = \vec{r} \times \vec{F} \)     \( |\vec{\tau}| = rF \sin \theta = F \times \text{perpendicular distance from axis} \)
Unit: N·m. Not same as Joule even though dimensions [ML²T⁻²] are same. Torque is a vector (axial/pseudovector).
Angular momentum and Newton's 2nd law for rotation
\( \vec{L} = \vec{r} \times \vec{p} = I\vec{\omega} \)     (magnitude: \( L = mvr \sin \theta = I\omega \))
\( \vec{\tau}_{\text{net}} = \frac{d\vec{L}}{dt} = I\vec{\alpha} \)     (rotational analog of \( F=ma \))
Conservation of angular momentum: if τ_ext = 0, then L = Iω = constant. This is why a spinning skater speeds up on pulling arms in (I decreases, ω increases).
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Moment of Inertia — All NEET-Important Values
Read Detailed Explanation

Moment of inertia is the rotational analog of mass, representing a body's resistance to angular acceleration. It depends fundamentally on the mass and its distribution relative to the axis of rotation. The Parallel Axis Theorem and Perpendicular Axis Theorem are crucial boundary tools for finding it about non-standard axes.

BodyAxisI (moment of inertia)
Thin rod (mass M, length L)Through center, ⊥ rodML²/12
Thin rod (mass M, length L)Through one end, ⊥ rodML²/3
Solid disk / cylinder (mass M, radius R)Central axis (perpendicular)MR²/2
Hollow cylinder / ring (mass M, radius R)Central axisMR²
Solid sphere (mass M, radius R)Diameter2MR²/5
Hollow sphere / spherical shell (mass M, radius R)Diameter2MR²/3
Rectangular plate (M, sides a×b)Through center, ⊥ plateM(a²+b²)/12
Parallel axis theorem & Perpendicular axis theorem
Parallel: \( I = I_{cm} + Md^2 \) (d = distance from CM to new axis)
Perpendicular: \( I_z = I_x + I_y \) (only for laminar/flat objects, \( z \perp \) plane)

Rolling Motion (Without Slipping)
Read Detailed Explanation

Rolling without slipping is a combined translational and rotational motion where the point of contact with the surface is instantaneously at rest. The friction involved is static, doing zero work, which allows the conservation of mechanical energy. This is a critical boundary condition for solving rolling on inclined planes.

Rolling = Rotation + Translation
Condition: \( v_{cm} = R\omega \) (no slipping)
Total KE: \( \frac{1}{2}Mv^2 + \frac{1}{2}I\omega^2 = \frac{1}{2}Mv^2 \left(1 + \frac{I}{MR^2}\right) = \frac{1}{2}Mv^2\left(1 + \frac{K^2}{R^2}\right) \)
For solid sphere: \( \frac{K^2}{R^2} = \frac{2}{5} \rightarrow \quad KE_{total} = \frac{7}{10} Mv^2 \)
For hollow sphere: \( \frac{K^2}{R^2} = \frac{2}{3} \rightarrow \quad KE_{total} = \frac{5}{6} Mv^2 \)
For disk/cylinder: \( \frac{K^2}{R^2} = \frac{1}{2} \rightarrow \quad KE_{total} = \frac{3}{4} Mv^2 \)
For ring: \( \frac{K^2}{R^2} = 1 \rightarrow \quad KE_{total} = Mv^2 \)
Rolling speed on incline: v = √(2gh/(1+K²/R²)). Solid sphere rolls fastest (smallest K²/R²). Ring rolls slowest. Solid sphere always reaches bottom before hollow sphere, disk, or ring.

Read Detailed Explanation

Practicing these problems helps consolidate rolling motion concepts. Pay special attention to the direction of static friction, which acts to prevent slipping. For NEET, compare the acceleration and velocity of different geometries like solid spheres, cylinders, and rings rolling down inclines.

Assertion-Reason — Rotational Motion
Read Detailed Explanation

Assertion-Reason questions evaluate deep theoretical clarity in rotational mechanics. To solve them, verify the assertion independently, then check if the reason is a scientifically valid statement. Finally, determine if the reason correctly explains the underlying physics, like why a skater pulls their arms in.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1 · Moment of inertia
Assertion: A solid sphere has higher angular velocity than a hollow sphere when both roll down the same incline from rest.
Reason: A solid sphere has smaller moment of inertia (2MR²/5) compared to a hollow sphere (2MR²/3) for same mass and radius.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. v_bottom = √(2gh/(1+K²/R²)). For solid sphere: K²/R²=2/5; hollow sphere: K²/R²=2/3. Solid sphere has smaller K²/R², so larger denominator is smaller, giving larger v (and hence ω=v/R). R is correct: smaller I means less rotational inertia → accelerates faster.
AR-2 · Angular momentum
Assertion: When a diver jumps from a diving board and tucks (brings knees to chest), his angular velocity increases.
Reason: On tucking, the moment of inertia decreases, and angular momentum being conserved, angular velocity increases.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. In the air, gravity acts through the CM, producing no torque about the CM. So L = Iω = constant. Tucking reduces I (mass closer to axis), so ω must increase. R is the perfect explanation using L conservation.
AR-3 · Torque
Assertion: Torque is zero when force passes through the axis of rotation (pivot point).
Reason: Torque = r × F, and r = 0 when force acts at the axis, so τ = 0.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. τ = r×F. If force acts at the axis (pivot), then r=0 → τ=0. More generally, torque = perpendicular distance from axis × force = 0 when force's line of action passes through axis. This is why pushing a door at its hinge has no effect.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Use these practice questions to master the interplay of torque, angular momentum, and rotational kinetic energy. For NEET, prioritize understanding conceptual edge cases, such as the minimum friction required for pure rolling or the tipping point of a block subjected to a force.

Q4 · Torque
A force of 10 N acts tangentially on a wheel of radius 0.5 m. Torque about central axis is:
a) 5 N·m
b) 20 N·m
c) 0.5 N·m
d) 2 N·m
Tangential force means it's perpendicular to the radius, so θ = 90°. τ = rF sinθ = 0.5×10×sin90° = 5 N·m. Torque is maximum when force is tangential (perpendicular to radius). Torque is zero when force is radial (along radius).
7

Gravitation

⭐ NEET Weight: 2–3 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 7 | Universal law, gravitational field, potential, satellites, Kepler's laws, escape velocity

Universal Law of Gravitation
Read Detailed Explanation

Newton's Universal Law states that every point mass attracts every other point mass with a force directly proportional to the product of their masses and inversely proportional to the square of their separation. This is a conservative, central force. The Superposition Principle strictly applies when calculating the net force.

Newton's Law of Gravitation
\( F = \frac{Gm_1m_2}{r^2} \quad G = 6.674 \times 10^{-11} \text{ N}\cdot\text{m}^2\cdot\text{kg}^{-2} \)
Always attractive. Acts along the line joining the two masses. Inverse square law — same as Coulomb's law but only attractive. G is universal constant (same everywhere). [G] = [M⁻¹L³T⁻²]
Variation of g with height (h), depth (d), latitude (\lambda )
\( g = \frac{GM}{R^2} = 9.8 \text{ m/s}^2 \) (at surface)
At height h: \( g_h = g\left(\frac{R}{R+h}\right)^2 \approx g\left(1 - \frac{2h}{R}\right) \) for \( h \ll R \)
At depth d: \( g_d = g\left(1 - \frac{d}{R}\right) \rightarrow \) at centre (\( d=R \)): \( g=0 \)
At latitude \( \lambda \): \( g_\lambda = g - R\omega^2\cos^2\lambda \rightarrow \) max at poles (\( \lambda=90^\circ \)), min at equator (\( \lambda=0^\circ \))
g decreases with height and depth both. g is maximum at poles, minimum at equator (due to Earth's rotation and oblate shape). At Earth's centre, g = 0.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Gravitation practice questions often involve balancing forces or finding neutral points where net force is zero. For NEET, visualize the vector nature of the gravitational force. Use symmetry to simplify calculations for regular polygons and spherical mass distributions.

Gravitational Field, Potential & Energy
Read Detailed Explanation

The gravitational field is the force experienced per unit mass, while gravitational potential is the work done per unit mass to bring it from infinity to a point. Because gravity is attractive, potential energy is always negative, maximizing at zero at infinity. The gradient relationship between potential and field is an essential principle.

Gravitational field intensity (E or g)
\( g = \frac{F}{m} = \frac{GM}{r^2} \) (due to mass M at distance r) [directed toward M]
Gravitational potential (V)
\( V = -\frac{GM}{r} \) (negative; increases toward zero as \( r \rightarrow \infty \))
Gravitational PE: \( U = mV = -\frac{GMm}{r} \)
Relation: \( g = -\frac{dV}{dr} \)
Gravitational potential is always negative (bound system). At infinity V=0. Work done to bring mass from ∞ to r = U = −GMm/r (negative means energy released).

Escape Velocity, Orbital Velocity & Satellites
Read Detailed Explanation

Orbital velocity is the speed required to keep a satellite in stable circular orbit, balancing gravitational pull with centripetal requirement. Escape velocity is the minimum speed needed at the surface to overcome a planet's gravitational binding energy. Crucially, escape velocity is independent of the projected body's mass and projection angle.

Escape velocity — minimum speed to escape Earth's gravity
\( v_e = \sqrt{\frac{2GM}{R}} = \sqrt{2gR} = R\sqrt{\frac{2g}{R}} \)
\( v_e = 11.2 \text{ km/s} \) (Earth), \( 2.38 \text{ km/s} \) (Moon), \( 617 \text{ km/s} \) (Sun)
Direction-independent (can escape in any direction at this speed). v_e = √2 × v_orbital (at surface). Moon has no atmosphere because gas molecules have speeds > v_e(moon).
Orbital velocity of satellite at height h
\( v_o = \sqrt{\frac{GM}{R+h}} = \sqrt{g_h(R+h)} \approx \sqrt{gR} \) for \( h \ll R \approx 7.9 \text{ km/s} \)
Time period: \( T = \frac{2\pi (R+h)}{v_o} = 2\pi\sqrt{\frac{(R+h)^3}{GM}} \)
Orbital velocity DECREASES as height increases (v_o ∝ 1/√r). Time period INCREASES with height (T ∝ r^(3/2) — Kepler's 3rd law!). Geostationary orbit: T = 24 h, height ≈ 36,000 km above equator.
Energy of satellite in orbit
\( KE = \frac{GMm}{2r} \quad PE = -\frac{GMm}{r} \quad \text{Total } E = -\frac{GMm}{2r} \)
Total \( E = -KE = \frac{1}{2} PE \)
Total energy is NEGATIVE (bound orbit). As satellite goes to higher orbit (more r): KE decreases, PE increases (less negative), \text{Total } E increases (less negative). Binding energy = −\text{Total } E = GMm/2r.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

These questions focus on the energy conservation of satellites and projectiles. For NEET, master the relationship between kinetic, potential, and total energy in an orbit. Always remember that mechanical energy remains conserved in orbit transitions.

Kepler's Laws of Planetary Motion
Read Detailed Explanation

Kepler's laws describe planetary orbits fundamentally. The Law of Orbits states planets move in ellipses. The Law of Areas arises from the conservation of angular momentum, implying planets move faster when closer to the sun. The Law of Periods relates the square of the orbital period to the cube of the semi-major axis.

1st Law — Law of Orbits

Every planet moves in an elliptical orbit with the Sun at one focus. Not circular! Perihelion = closest point; Aphelion = farthest point.

2nd Law — Law of Areas

The line joining planet to Sun sweeps equal areas in equal times. Planet moves faster at perihelion, slower at aphelion. This is conservation of angular momentum!

3rd Law — Law of Periods

T² ∝ a³ where a = semi-major axis.
T₁²/T₂² = a₁³/a₂³
For circular orbit: T² ∝ R³. Direct consequence of Newton's law of gravitation.

Assertion-Reason — Gravitation
Read Detailed Explanation

In Gravitation A-R questions, focus on dependencies. Does escape velocity depend on the mass of the object? No. Does gravitational potential change inside a solid sphere? Yes. Carefully trace the logical link between the assertion and the reason, ensuring accurate physical causation.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: An astronaut in a freely orbiting spacecraft experiences weightlessness.
Reason: Gravity does not act on the astronaut when the spacecraft is in orbit.
ABCDE
Answer: (C) — A is TRUE, R is FALSE. Gravity DOES act on the astronaut — it's what keeps the spacecraft in orbit. The astronaut experiences weightlessness because BOTH the spacecraft and astronaut are in free fall together (both have same acceleration = g_orbital). The reason is wrong: gravity acts, but there's no normal force because everything falls together. This is "apparent weightlessness," not true zero gravity.
AR-2
Assertion: A planet moves faster when it is closer to the Sun (at perihelion) than when it is farther (at aphelion).
Reason: The angular momentum of the planet about the Sun is conserved.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. Gravity is a central force (along r), so torque about Sun = 0, meaning L = mvr = constant. At perihelion (small r), v must be large. At aphelion (large r), v is small. This is Kepler's 2nd law — equal areas in equal times — which is a direct consequence of angular momentum conservation.
AR-3
Assertion: The gravitational potential energy of a system of two masses is always negative.
Reason: Gravitational force is always attractive, so work done by gravity in bringing masses together is negative.
ABCDE
Answer: (C) — A is TRUE, R is FALSE. A is correct: U = −GMm/r which is always negative (taking U=0 at infinity). But R is wrong: when gravity brings masses together (r decreases), work by gravity is POSITIVE (force and displacement in same direction). The negative PE arises from the CHOICE of reference (U=0 at infinity), not from the sign of work. When gravity does positive work, PE decreases (becomes more negative).
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Gravitation conceptual questions often test your understanding of Kepler's laws and variations in acceleration due to gravity. For NEET, recall that 'g' decreases with both altitude and depth. Be mindful of boundary conditions like zero gravity at the earth's center or the effect of Earth's rotation at the equator.

Q3 · Kepler's 3rd law
If the orbital period of a planet is 8 years, its mean distance from Sun compared to Earth (1 AU) is:
a) 2 AU
b) 8 AU
c) 64 AU
d) 4 AU
T² ∝ a³. (T/T_earth)² = (a/1 AU)³. (8/1)² = a³ → 64 = a³ → a = 4 AU. So the planet is 4 times farther than Earth from the Sun. Check: T=8y → T² = 64; a=4 AU → a³ = 64 ✓. This is exactly Kepler's 3rd law.
8

Thermal Properties of Matter

⭐ NEET Weight: 2–3 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 8 | Thermal expansion, calorimetry, heat transfer, Newton's law of cooling

Thermal Expansion
Read Detailed Explanation

Thermal expansion is the tendency of matter to change its shape, area, and volume in response to a change in temperature. It fundamentally arises from the asymmetric nature of the interatomic potential energy curve. As thermal energy increases, the average separation between atoms increases. Water exhibits anomalous expansion between 0°C and 4°C.

Linear, Area, and Volume expansion
Linear: \( \Delta L = \alpha L\Delta T \quad L = L_0 (1 + \alpha \Delta T) \)
Area: \( \Delta A = \beta A\Delta T = 2\alpha A\Delta T \quad [\beta = 2\alpha] \)
Volume: \( \Delta V = \gamma V\Delta T = 3\alpha V\Delta T \quad [\gamma = 3\alpha] \)
α = coefficient of linear expansion (°C⁻¹ or K⁻¹). \beta = 2α. \gamma = 3α. For liquids: only volumetric expansion (no shape). Apparent expansion of liquid \gamma _apparent = \gamma _liquid − \gamma _container.
Anomalous Expansion of Water — NEET Important

Water contracts on heating from 0°C to 4°C (density increases). At 4°C density is maximum (1000 kg/m³). Above 4°C, water expands normally. This anomalous behavior is responsible for aquatic life surviving in frozen lakes — water at 4°C sinks to bottom, ice floats on top.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Practice questions on thermal expansion often test the application of linear, areal, and volume expansion coefficients. For NEET, focus on the proportionality relationship of expansion coefficients for isotropic materials. Remember to account for the apparent expansion of liquids when heated in a container.

Calorimetry
Read Detailed Explanation

Calorimetry is the science of measuring heat transfer during physical changes or phase transitions. It is governed by the Principle of Calorimetry: Heat lost by hot bodies equals heat gained by cold bodies in an isolated system. Boundary conditions require careful tracking of latent heat during phase changes.

Heat exchange in calorimetry (no heat loss)
\( Q = mc\Delta T \) (sensible heat — temperature changes)
\( Q = mL \) (latent heat — phase change, temperature CONSTANT)
Heat lost = Heat gained (principle of calorimetry)
c_water = 4200 J kg⁻¹K⁻¹ = 1 cal/g°C. L_fusion(ice) = 3.34×10⁵ J/kg = 80 cal/g. L_vaporisation(water) = 22.6×10⁵ J/kg = 540 cal/g. 1 cal = 4.2 J (mechanical equivalent of heat).
Common Mistake — Mixing Ice and Water

When ice at 0°C is mixed with water at T°C: First, determine if all ice melts. Calculate Q_needed to melt all ice = m_ice × L_f. Calculate Q_available from water cooling to 0°C = m_water × c × T. If Q_available > Q_needed: all ice melts, solve for final T above 0°C. If Q_available < Q_needed: ice is not completely melted, final T = 0°C, find mass of ice melted.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Calorimetry problems in NEET require setting up a strict heat balance equation. Always identify the final equilibrium state, especially if a phase change (like ice melting in water) might only partially complete. Check if the available heat is sufficient to overcome the total latent heat boundary.
