Heat Transfer — Three Modes
Read Detailed Explanation

Heat transfers via Conduction, Convection, and Radiation. Conduction relies on direct molecular collisions and free electrons. Convection involves actual bulk movement of fluids due to density differences. Radiation requires no medium, transferring energy via electromagnetic waves, governed heavily by the Stefan-Boltzmann Law and Wien's Displacement Law.

Conduction

Heat flows through solid without bulk motion. Rate: dQ/dt = KA(T₁−T₂)/L where K = thermal conductivity, A = area, L = length. Thermal resistance R = L/KA. Series: R_total = R₁+R₂. Parallel: 1/R_total = 1/R₁+1/R₂.

Convection

Heat transfer by bulk movement of fluid. Natural convection: due to density differences (hot air rises). Forced convection: fan, pump. Responsible for sea breeze, land breeze, trade winds, ocean currents. Cannot occur in vacuum.

Radiation

Heat transfer via electromagnetic waves. No medium needed. Stefan-Boltzmann: P = σεAT⁴ where σ = 5.67×10⁻⁸ W/m²K⁴. ε = emissivity (0 to 1; black body ε=1). Net power: P = σεA(T⁴ − T₀⁴).

Newton's Law of Cooling & Wien's Displacement Law
Newton's Cooling: \( \frac{dT}{dt} = -k(T - T_0) \) [valid for small \( \Delta T \)]
Average: \( \frac{T_1-T_2}{t} = k\left(\frac{T_1+T_2}{2} - T_0\right) \)
Wien's Displacement: \( \lambda_{max} T = b = 2.9\times 10^{-3} \text{ m}\cdot\text{K} \)
Wien's law: hotter objects emit at shorter wavelengths. Sun (5778K): \lambda _max ≈ 500 nm (visible). Human body (310K): \lambda _max ≈ 9400 nm (infrared).
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Heat transfer questions often compare the rates of cooling or thermal resistances. For NEET, treat steady-state conduction like electrical circuits with thermal resistance. For radiation, apply Newton's Law of Cooling as a reliable approximation when temperature differences are small.

Assertion-Reason — Thermal Properties
Read Detailed Explanation

Assertion-Reason questions here test intricate details like why cooking is faster in a pressure cooker or why a thick glass cracks when hot water is poured. Evaluate the underlying principle—whether it involves specific heat capacity, latent heat, or anomalous expansion—before linking the reason to the assertion.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: A body good at absorbing radiation is also good at emitting radiation.
Reason: A black body (perfect absorber) is also the best emitter of radiation at all wavelengths.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. This is Kirchhoff's Law of radiation: emissivity = absorptivity at any given wavelength and temperature. A perfect absorber (black body, ε=1) is also the perfect emitter. R correctly gives the limiting case: a black body has maximum absorptivity AND maximum emissivity. Hence A is true.
AR-2
Assertion: Water kept in an earthen pot remains cool even in summer.
Reason: Earthen pot is a good conductor of heat, so heat flows into the pot rapidly.
ABCDE
Answer: (C) — A is TRUE, R is FALSE. Water stays cool due to EVAPORATION through pores (evaporative cooling — latent heat of vaporization). The earthen pot is porous, allowing water to seep out and evaporate, carrying heat away. The reason is wrong: earthen pots are poor conductors (good insulators). The cooling mechanism is phase change (evaporation), not conduction.
AR-3
Assertion: During a phase change (melting or boiling), temperature remains constant even though heat is being supplied.
Reason: The heat supplied during phase change is used to overcome intermolecular forces, not to increase kinetic energy.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. Temperature = average kinetic energy. During phase change, heat goes into increasing potential energy (overcoming intermolecular attractions to change state) not kinetic energy. Since KE doesn't increase, temperature doesn't change. R is the correct molecular-level explanation of latent heat.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Use these questions to strengthen your intuition on thermal properties. For NEET, differentiate clearly between thermal conductivity (a material property) and thermal capacity (an object property). Remember that a perfect black body is both an ideal absorber and an ideal emitter.

Q3 · Stefan's law
If the temperature of a black body is doubled, the rate of radiation becomes:
a) 2 times
b) 4 times
c) 8 times
d) 16 times
P = σAT⁴. If T → 2T: P_new = σA(2T)⁴ = 16σAT⁴ = 16P. So radiation becomes 16 times. Stefan's law has T⁴ — one of the most common NEET applications. T doubles → P increases 2⁴=16 fold. Similarly, T triples → P increases 81 fold.
Q4 · Newton's law of cooling
A body cools from 80°C to 60°C in 5 min. Room temperature is 20°C. Time to cool from 60°C to 40°C is approximately:
a) 10 min
b) 5 min
c) 7 min
d) 15 min
Using average form: (80−60)/5 = k[(80+60)/2 − 20] → 4 = k[70−20] = 50k → k = 4/50 = 0.08 min⁻¹. Now: (60−40)/t = k[(60+40)/2 − 20] = 0.08×[50−20] = 0.08×30 = 2.4. So t = 20/2.4 ≈ 8.3 min. But the standard answer is approximately 10 min because as the body cools further, the rate slows more. The temperature difference from room shrinks faster for the 60→40 interval.
9

Thermodynamics

⭐ NEET Weight: 3–4 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 9 | Laws of thermodynamics, processes, entropy, Carnot engine, heat engines

Laws of Thermodynamics
Read Detailed Explanation

The Zeroth Law defines temperature and thermal equilibrium. The First Law is the conservation of energy, showing heat supplied changes internal energy or does work. The Second Law dictates the direction of heat flow and asserts that no heat engine can have 100% efficiency, introducing the concept of entropy.

Zeroth Law

If A is in thermal equilibrium with C, and B is in thermal equilibrium with C, then A and B are in thermal equilibrium with each other. This law defines temperature as a thermodynamic property.

Third Law

Entropy of a perfectly crystalline substance at absolute zero (0 K) is zero. This allows calculation of absolute entropies and explains why absolute zero is unattainable.

First Law of Thermodynamics (Conservation of energy)
First Law: \( \Delta U = Q - W \) (Q = heat added to system, W = work done BY system)
Work Done: \( W = \int p \, dV = p\Delta V \) (for constant pressure) = area under p-V curve
Enthalpy: \( \Delta H = \Delta U + p\Delta V = Q_p \)
Sign convention (NCERT): Q positive when heat absorbed by system; W positive when work done BY system. ΔU depends only on initial and final states (state function). Q and W depend on path (path functions).
Second Law of Thermodynamics
Kelvin-Planck: No heat engine can convert all heat into work (\( \eta < 100\% \))
Clausius: Heat cannot flow spontaneously from cold to hot body
Entropy: \( \Delta S = \frac{Q_{rev}}{T} \ge 0 \) for any process (\( \Delta S_{universe} \ge 0 \))

Thermodynamic Processes — Complete Comparison
Read Detailed Explanation

Thermodynamic processes describe how a system transitions between states. Isothermal occurs at constant temperature. Adiabatic implies no heat exchange. Isobaric means constant pressure, and Isochoric means constant volume, hence zero work done. The area under a P-V curve always yields the boundary work.

ProcessConditionQW = p·ΔVΔUIdeal Gas
IsothermalT = const= WnRT ln(V₂/V₁)0pV = const → hyperbola
AdiabaticQ = 00= −ΔU= −WpV\gamma = const → steeper than isothermal
Isobaricp = const= nCpΔTpΔV = nRΔT= nCvΔTV/T = const (Charles' law)
IsochoricV = const= ΔU0= nCvΔTp/T = const (Gay-Lussac's law)
CyclicReturn to start= W= area enclosed0Efficiency η = W/Q_H
Adiabatic relations (important for NEET)
Equations: \( pV^\gamma = \text{const}, \quad TV^{\gamma-1} = \text{const}, \quad T^\gamma p^{1-\gamma} = \text{const} \)
Gamma (\( \gamma \)): \( \gamma = \frac{C_p}{C_v} = \frac{f+2}{f} \) where f = degrees of freedom
Values: Monatomic (\( f=3, \gamma=5/3 \)), Diatomic (\( f=5, \gamma=7/5 \)), Triatomic (\( f=6, \gamma=4/3 \))

Heat Engines & Carnot Cycle
Read Detailed Explanation

A heat engine converts heat from a hot reservoir into work, rejecting the rest to a cold reservoir. The Carnot Cycle is an ideal, reversible cycle (two isothermal, two adiabatic processes) providing the maximum theoretical efficiency. It establishes the boundary condition that efficiency depends solely on reservoir temperatures.

Efficiency of heat engine and Carnot engine
Efficiency: \( \eta = \frac{W}{Q_H} = 1 - \frac{Q_C}{Q_H} = 1 - \frac{T_C}{T_H} \) (Carnot — maximum possible)
COP of refrigerator: \( COP = \frac{Q_C}{W} = \frac{T_C}{T_H - T_C} \)
COP of heat pump: \( COP = \frac{Q_H}{W} = \frac{T_H}{T_H - T_C} \)
Carnot efficiency depends only on temperatures (in Kelvin). Any real engine has η < η_Carnot. To increase efficiency: increase T_H or decrease T_C. η_Carnot = 1 only if T_C = 0 K (unattainable).

Carnot Cycle — 4 Steps

  1. Isothermal expansion at T_H (absorbs Q_H)
  2. Adiabatic expansion (T_H → T_C)
  3. Isothermal compression at T_C (rejects Q_C)
  4. Adiabatic compression (T_C → T_H)

Spontaneity — ΔG Rules

ΔHΔSSpontaneous?
−+✅ Always
−−At low T only
++At high T only
+−❌ Never
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Thermodynamics practice questions heavily rely on the First Law and P-V graphs. For NEET, focus on the sign conventions: work done by the gas is positive, heat added is positive. Quickly identify cyclic processes where the net change in internal energy is precisely zero.

Assertion-Reason — Thermodynamics
Read Detailed Explanation

In Thermodynamics A-R questions, look for violations of the First or Second Laws. Understand that internal energy is a state function, completely independent of the path taken, whereas work and heat are path-dependent. Verify if the reason correctly justifies adiabatic versus isothermal processes.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: In an adiabatic process, no heat is exchanged between the system and surroundings, yet the temperature of the system can change.
Reason: In an adiabatic process, work done by or on the gas changes its internal energy, hence temperature.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. In adiabatic: Q=0, so ΔU = −W. If gas expands (W positive), ΔU is negative → T decreases. If compressed (W negative, work done ON gas), ΔU is positive → T increases. R correctly uses the First Law to explain temperature change without heat transfer. Example: adiabatic compression in diesel engine ignites fuel by heating alone.
AR-2
Assertion: Efficiency of a Carnot engine can be 100% if the sink temperature is 0 K.
Reason: The Third Law of Thermodynamics states that absolute zero (0 K) is unattainable.
ABCDE
Answer: (B) — Both are true but R does NOT explain A. A is true: η = 1−T_C/T_H = 1−0/T_H = 1 = 100% if T_C = 0 K mathematically. R is also true: Third Law says 0 K cannot be reached. However, R explains WHY this 100% efficiency is practically impossible, making it an indirect explanation, not a direct one. This is a classic B-type assertion-reason: both facts are correct but the reasoning link is indirect/incomplete.
AR-3
Assertion: In an isothermal expansion of an ideal gas, the internal energy remains unchanged.
Reason: Internal energy of an ideal gas depends only on its temperature.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. For an ideal gas, U = nCvT. Isothermal means T = constant → ΔU = 0. R is the fundamental reason: no intermolecular PE in ideal gas, so U is purely translational KE which depends only on T. Therefore isothermal → ΔT=0 → ΔU=0, and by First Law: Q = W (all heat absorbed converts to work).
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

These questions solidify your grasp on efficiency, process identification, and energy conservation. For NEET, always convert temperatures to Kelvin when calculating Carnot efficiency or applying gas laws. Master the interpretation of indicator diagrams to quickly find work done.

Q1 · First law application
1000 J of heat is supplied to a system and it does 400 J of work. Change in internal energy is:
a) 400 J
b) 600 J
c) 1400 J
d) −600 J
ΔU = Q − W = 1000 − 400 = 600 J. System absorbs 1000 J; uses 400 J to do work; remaining 600 J stored as internal energy (increases molecular KE → temperature rises). First Law: energy is conserved — Q in = W done + ΔU stored.
Q3 · Isothermal vs adiabatic
An ideal gas expands from V to 2V. Work done in isothermal expansion is W₁ and in adiabatic expansion is W₂. Which is correct?
a) W₁ = W₂
b) W₁ < W₂
c) W₁ = 2W₂
d) W₁ > W₂
In isothermal expansion, temperature is maintained (heat absorbed from surroundings), so pressure stays higher throughout. In adiabatic expansion, temperature falls (no heat input), so pressure drops faster. The p-V curve for adiabatic is steeper. Area under isothermal curve > area under adiabatic curve → W₁ > W₂. Isothermal does more work for same volume change.
Q4 · Internal energy
For an ideal gas, internal energy depends on:
a) Temperature only
b) Pressure only
c) Volume only
d) Both pressure and volume
For an IDEAL gas, U = nCvT — depends only on temperature. This is because ideal gas molecules have no intermolecular forces (no potential energy), so U is purely kinetic. In isothermal process (T=const), ΔU=0 regardless of pressure or volume changes. Real gases do have U depending on volume (intermolecular PE).
10

Kinetic Theory of Gases

⭐ NEET Weight: 2–3 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 10 | Ideal gas assumptions, pressure derivation, molecular speeds, equipartition theorem

Ideal Gas Laws & Assumptions
Read Detailed Explanation

The kinetic theory models a gas as identical, point-like molecules in continuous random motion, with completely elastic collisions and no intermolecular forces. This gives rise to the Ideal Gas Law. A critical boundary condition is that real gases approximate ideal behavior only at high temperatures and low pressures.

Ideal Gas Equation
Macroscopic: \( pV = nRT \) (n = moles, \( R = 8.314 \text{ J}\cdot\text{mol}^{-1}\cdot\text{K}^{-1} \))
Microscopic: \( pV = NkT \) (N = number of molecules, \( k = 1.38 \times 10^{-23} \text{ J/K} \) = Boltzmann constant)
Relation: \( k = \frac{R}{N_A} \)
Assumptions: molecules are point masses (no volume), no intermolecular forces, all collisions elastic, molecules in random motion, large number of molecules (statistical treatment valid).
Pressure of ideal gas (derived from kinetic theory)
Pressure: \( p = \frac{1}{3}\rho v_{rms}^2 = \frac{1}{3}\left(\frac{Nm}{V}\right)v_{rms}^2 = \frac{2}{3}\left(\frac{N}{V}\right)\left(\frac{1}{2}mv_{rms}^2\right) \)
Total KE: \( pV = \frac{2}{3}N\left(\frac{1}{2}mv_{rms}^2\right) = \frac{2}{3}KE_{total} \)
This derivation shows: pressure is a macroscopic manifestation of molecular motion. Higher T → higher molecular KE → higher pressure (at same volume).

Molecular Speeds — Three Types
Read Detailed Explanation

Gas molecules exhibit a distribution of speeds governed by Maxwell-Boltzmann statistics. The Root Mean Square speed dictates kinetic energy. The Average speed is the arithmetic mean, while the Most Probable speed is the peak of the distribution curve. Fundamentally, all speeds scale with the square root of temperature over molar mass.

Three characteristic molecular speeds
RMS speed: \( v_{rms} = \sqrt{\frac{3RT}{M}} = \sqrt{\frac{3kT}{m}} \) [M = molar mass in kg/mol]
Mean speed: \( v_{mean} = \sqrt{\frac{8RT}{\pi M}} = \sqrt{\frac{8kT}{\pi m}} \)
Most probable speed: \( v_{mp} = \sqrt{\frac{2RT}{M}} = \sqrt{\frac{2kT}{m}} \)
Order: v_mp < v_mean < v_rms  |  Ratio: v_mp : v_mean : v_rms = √2 : √(8/π) : √3 ≈ 1 : 1.128 : 1.225
All speeds ∝ √T (double T → speed increases by √2). All ∝ 1/√M (heavier molecule → slower).
Average kinetic energy per molecule
Per molecule: \( KE_{avg} = \frac{3}{2}kT \) (monatomic — all translational)
Per mole: \( KE_{avg} = \frac{3}{2}RT \)
This is the most important result: average KE per molecule = (3/2)kT for any ideal gas (independent of mass!). At same temperature, all molecules have same average KE, but lighter ones move faster.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

KTG practice questions frequently test the dependence of molecular speeds and pressure on temperature and mass. For NEET, remember the strict relative order of RMS, average, and most probable speeds. Ensure you use molar mass in kilograms when using SI units to calculate exact speeds.

Law of Equipartition of Energy & Degrees of Freedom
Read Detailed Explanation

Degrees of freedom represent the independent ways a molecule can possess energy. The Law of Equipartition of Energy states that in thermal equilibrium, every active degree of freedom contributes equally to the total energy. This fundamentally determines the specific heat capacities of gases.

Molecule TypeDegrees of freedom (f)U per moleCvCp\gamma = Cp/Cv
Monatomic (He, Ar, Ne)3 (translational)(3/2)RT(3/2)R(5/2)R5/3 = 1.67
Diatomic (H₂, O₂, N₂) at moderate T5 (3 trans + 2 rot)(5/2)RT(5/2)R(7/2)R7/5 = 1.4
Triatomic linear (CO₂)7 (3+2+2 vib)(7/2)RT(7/2)R(9/2)R9/7 ≈ 1.29
Triatomic non-linear (H₂O)6 (3+3)3RT3R4R4/3 ≈ 1.33
Equipartition Theorem

Each degree of freedom contributes ½kT of kinetic energy per molecule (= ½RT per mole). Total U = (f/2)nRT. Cp − Cv = R always (for ideal gas). This is Mayer's relation.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Equipartition problems require you to classify gases and assign degrees of freedom. For NEET, remember that vibrational modes only activate at significantly high temperatures. Use degrees of freedom to quickly calculate the adiabatic index for mixture problems.

Assertion-Reason — Kinetic Theory
Read Detailed Explanation

A-R questions in KTG test the foundational postulates. If an assertion claims pressure is due to intermolecular repulsion, it's false; pressure arises from the momentum change during elastic wall collisions. Always verify whether the scenario involves an ideal or real gas to evaluate the reason correctly.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: Lighter gas molecules diffuse faster than heavier ones at the same temperature.
Reason: At the same temperature, all gas molecules have the same average kinetic energy.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. Since ½mv² = (3/2)kT (same for all), v ∝ 1/√m. Lighter molecules have higher v → diffuse faster (Graham's law of diffusion). R is the fundamental reason: same KE but lighter mass → higher speed → faster diffusion. Rate of diffusion ∝ 1/√M (Graham's law).
AR-2
Assertion: Real gases deviate from ideal behavior at high pressure and low temperature.
Reason: At high pressure and low temperature, intermolecular forces and molecular volumes become significant.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. Ideal gas assumes: no volume for molecules, no intermolecular forces. At high pressure: molecules are close → their volumes matter → pV > nRT. At low T: slow molecules → attractive forces important → pV < nRT. Van der Waals equation (p+a/V²)(V−b)=nRT corrects for these. R correctly identifies both corrections needed.
AR-3
Assertion: The value of Cp − Cv is the same for all ideal gases.
Reason: Cp − Cv = R (Mayer's relation), which is a universal gas constant independent of the nature of gas.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. Cp − Cv = R = 8.314 J mol⁻¹K⁻¹ for all ideal gases, regardless of atomicity. The extra work to expand against pressure at constant p (the pΔV = nRΔT term) is always R per mole per K — same for monatomic, diatomic, or any ideal gas. R is R!
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Mastering KTG requires applying microscopic models to macroscopic observables. For NEET, practice finding the internal energy of gas mixtures. Understand the fundamental concept of mean free path and its inverse dependence on pressure and the square of the molecular diameter.

Q1 · RMS speed
At what temperature is the rms speed of O₂ molecules equal to that of H₂ at 300 K? (M_O₂ = 32, M_H₂ = 2)
a) 300 K
b) 9600 K
c) 4800 K
d) 600 K
v_rms = √(3RT/M). For equal v_rms: T_O₂/M_O₂ = T_H₂/M_H₂ → T_O₂/32 = 300/2 → T_O₂ = 300×32/2 = 4800 K. O₂ needs much higher temperature to achieve same speed as H₂ (16× heavier → needs 16× temperature). This explains why H₂ and He escape Earth's atmosphere more easily.
Q4 · Pressure and temperature
The pressure of a gas is doubled while keeping volume constant. What happens to the rms speed of molecules?
a) Increases by √2
b) Doubles
c) Remains unchanged
d) Increases by factor 4
V = const, p doubles → T doubles (Gay-Lussac's law: p/T = const). v_rms = √(3RT/M) ∝ √T. T doubles → v_rms = √(2T/T) × original = √2 times original. Doubling pressure at constant volume doubles absolute temperature, which increases rms speed by √2.
11

Oscillations (SHM)

⭐ NEET Weight: 3–4 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 11 | Simple harmonic motion, energy, pendulums, springs, damped oscillations

SHM — Definition & Equations
Read Detailed Explanation

Simple Harmonic Motion (SHM) is a special periodic motion where the restoring force is directly proportional to the displacement from the mean position and directed towards it. It represents the projection of uniform circular motion on a diameter. The foundational acceleration equation is the defining boundary condition.

Simple Harmonic Motion — defining equation
Defining Equation: \( a = -\omega^2x \) (acceleration proportional to displacement, opposite direction)
Displacement (x): \( x = A \sin(\omega t + \phi) \) or \( x = A \cos(\omega t + \phi) \)
Velocity (v): \( v = A\omega \cos(\omega t + \phi) = \omega\sqrt{A^2-x^2} \)
Acceleration (a): \( a = -A\omega^2 \sin(\omega t + \phi) = -\omega^2x \)
A = amplitude, ω = angular frequency (rad/s), φ = initial phase, T = 2π/ω, f = ω/2π. At x=0: v=max=Aω, a=0. At x=±A: v=0, a=max=Aω².
Energy in SHM
Kinetic Energy (KE): \( KE = \frac{1}{2}m\omega^2(A^2-x^2) = \frac{1}{2}m\omega^2A^2\cos^2(\omega t) \)
Potential Energy (PE): \( PE = \frac{1}{2}m\omega^2x^2 = \frac{1}{2}m\omega^2A^2\sin^2(\omega t) \)
Total Energy (E): \( E = KE + PE = \frac{1}{2}m\omega^2A^2 = \frac{1}{2}kA^2 \) (constant, independent of position)
Total energy ∝ A² (square of amplitude). At mean position (x=0): all KE, no PE. At extreme (x=A): all PE, no KE. KE=PE when x = A/√2 (and speed = Aω/√2).
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

SHM practice questions focus on kinematic equations and energy conservation. For NEET, firmly memorize the phase relationships: velocity leads displacement by ninety degrees, and acceleration leads velocity by ninety degrees. Identify extreme and mean positions to quickly find max velocity and zero acceleration.

Standard SHM Systems
Read Detailed Explanation

Standard SHM includes the spring-mass system and the simple pendulum. Fundamentally, the time period is determined solely by the inertial factor and the spring factor. A key boundary condition is that pendulum SHM is only valid for small angular displacements.

SystemTime Period TRestoring ForceKey Notes
Spring-mass (horizontal)2π√(m/k)F = −kxIndependent of g; valid on Moon/space
Simple pendulum2π√(L/g)F = −mg sinθ ≈ −mgθValid only for small θ (<15°); T independent of mass
Springs in series2π√(m/k_eff)1/k_eff = 1/k₁+1/k₂Series: k_eff smaller → T larger
Springs in parallel2π√(m/k_eff)k_eff = k₁+k₂Parallel: k_eff larger → T smaller
Compound pendulum2π√(I/mgd)Torque = −mgd sinθd = distance from pivot to CM
Liquid in U-tube (length L)2π√(L/2g)Pressure differenceL = total length of liquid
Pendulum on Accelerating Lift / Different Gravity

T = 2π√(L/g_eff). In lift accelerating up at a: g_eff = g+a (T decreases, pendulum fast). In lift accelerating down at a: g_eff = g−a (T increases). In free fall: g_eff = 0 → T = ∞ (doesn't oscillate). On Moon (g_moon = g/6): T = √6 times Earth value.

Damped, Forced Oscillations & Resonance
Read Detailed Explanation

In real systems, non-conservative forces dissipate energy, causing damped oscillations where amplitude decays. In forced oscillations, an external periodic force maintains the motion. Resonance is the critical phenomenon where the external frequency matches the natural frequency, leading to maximum amplitude and energy transfer.

Damped Oscillations

Amplitude decreases exponentially with time due to resistive force (F = −bv). x = Ae^(−bt/2m)cos(ω't+φ). Effective ω' = √(k/m − b²/4m²). Total energy decreases exponentially.

Forced Oscillations

External periodic force drives the oscillator. After initial transient, system oscillates at driver frequency ω_d. Amplitude depends on (ω_d − ω₀) — maximum when ω_d = ω₀ (resonance).

Resonance

When driver frequency = natural frequency: amplitude → maximum. Less damping → sharper resonance peak → higher maximum amplitude. Examples: Tacoma bridge, MRI, radio tuning, microwave oven, shattering glass.

Assertion-Reason — SHM
Read Detailed Explanation

SHM A-R questions often explore energy graphs and phase differences. Verify concepts like whether total mechanical energy is always conserved (true only for undamped SHM). Be prepared to evaluate reasons explaining why a pendulum's time period changes in accelerating frames due to a change in effective gravity.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: Total mechanical energy in SHM remains constant throughout the motion.
Reason: Restoring force in SHM is conservative.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. The spring restoring force F=−kx is conservative (work done depends only on end points, not path). For conservative forces, mechanical energy (KE+PE) is conserved. R is the direct reason why A holds: if the force were non-conservative (like friction), energy would dissipate and the oscillation would be damped.
AR-2
Assertion: The time period of a simple pendulum does not depend on the mass of the bob.
Reason: The restoring force and the inertia both depend on mass, and their effects cancel.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. T = 2π√(L/g) — no mass! Restoring force = mg sinθ (∝ m), but by F=ma: acceleration = g sinθ (mass cancels). Since a is independent of m, T is independent of m. R precisely explains this cancellation — the same principle as Galileo's observation that all objects fall with same acceleration.
AR-3
Assertion: At resonance, the amplitude of forced oscillation can become very large.
Reason: At resonance, the driving frequency equals the natural frequency of the system.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. When ω_driving = ω₀ (natural frequency), energy is transferred most efficiently from driver to oscillator each cycle. In the absence of damping, amplitude → ∞ theoretically. In practice, damping limits amplitude to a finite (but large) value. R is the defining condition for resonance, which explains the large amplitude in A.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Practicing SHM concepts involves applying the restoring force condition and energy conservation. Focus on extreme and mean positions, analyzing kinetic and potential energy interplay, and correctly identifying the phase angle from initial conditions to master oscillatory motion.

Q1 · Time period of spring
A spring of force constant k is cut into two equal pieces. One piece is used with same mass m. New time period compared to original is:
a) Same T
b) T/√2
c) √2 × T
d) 2T
Cutting spring to half length → k_new = 2k (stiffer spring, same material and cross-section). T = 2π√(m/k). T_new = 2π√(m/2k) = T/√2. Spring constant is INVERSELY proportional to length. Cutting in half doubles k. Doubling k reduces T by factor √2.
Q2 · Energy in SHM
In SHM, at what displacement is KE equal to PE?
a) x = A
b) x = A/2
c) x = A/√2
d) x = 0
KE = PE → ½mω²(A²−x²) = ½mω²x² → A²−x² = x² → A² = 2x² → x = A/√2. At this position, each is ½ of total energy = ¼mω²A². The speed at this point = Aω/√2.
Q3 · Pendulum on Moon
A seconds pendulum (T=2s on Earth) is taken to Moon where g = g_E/6. Its period on Moon is:
a) 2 s
c) 12 s
c) 6 s
d) 2√6 s ≈ 4.9 s
T = 2π√(L/g). T_moon/T_earth = √(g_earth/g_moon) = √(6g_moon/g_moon) = √6. T_moon = 2√6 ≈ 4.9 s. A pendulum clock taken to Moon runs SLOWER (larger period) — it would lose time compared to Earth. This is because g is smaller on Moon → less restoring force → slower oscillation.
Q5 · Springs in parallel
Two springs of constant k each are connected in parallel and a mass m is attached. Time period is:
a) 2π√(m/k)
b) 2π√(m/2k)
c) 2π√(2m/k)
d) π√(m/k)
Parallel springs: k_eff = k+k = 2k. T = 2π√(m/k_eff) = 2π√(m/2k). With two springs in parallel, the system is stiffer → shorter period → faster oscillation. If series: k_eff = k/2, T = 2π√(2m/k) → longer period.
12

Waves

⭐ NEET Weight: 3–5 Questions

Class 11, Chapter 12 | Wave motion, speed of sound, superposition, interference, beats, standing waves, Doppler effect

Wave Equation & Basic Relations
Read Detailed Explanation

A wave transports energy without transporting matter. The wave equation links displacement with space and time. Fundamentally, wave speed depends entirely on the medium's elastic and inertial properties. A valid wave function must be finite everywhere and satisfy the differential wave equation.

Standard transverse wave equation
\( y(x,t) = A \sin (kx - \omega t) \) (wave moving in +x direction)
\( y(x,t) = A \sin (kx + \omega t) \) (wave moving in -x direction)
\( k = \frac{2\pi}{\lambda} \) (wave number), \( \omega = \frac{2\pi}{T} = 2\pi f \), \( v = \frac{\omega}{k} = f\lambda \)
Speed v = f\lambda = \lambda /T. Transverse: displacement ⊥ propagation (string, EM waves). Longitudinal: displacement ∥ propagation (sound, compression waves).
Speed of sound in different media
In solid: \( v = \sqrt{\frac{Y}{\rho}} \) [\( Y \) = Young's modulus]
In liquid: \( v = \sqrt{\frac{B}{\rho}} \) [\( B \) = bulk modulus]
In gas (Newton): \( v = \sqrt{\frac{p}{\rho}} = \sqrt{\frac{RT}{M}} \) (isothermal — incorrect)
In gas (Laplace): \( v = \sqrt{\frac{\gamma p}{\rho}} = \sqrt{\frac{\gamma RT}{M}} \) (adiabatic — correct!)
Laplace corrected Newton by noting sound propagation is adiabatic (too fast for heat exchange). v_sound ∝ √T → at 0°C: 332 m/s; at 20°C: 343 m/s. v_sound ∝ 1/√M (lighter gas → faster sound). Sound travels: solids > liquids > gases.

Superposition, Interference & Beats
Read Detailed Explanation

When two or more waves overlap, their net displacement is the vector sum of individual displacements (Superposition Principle). Interference is the spatial modification of intensity when coherent waves meet. Beats arise from the temporal interference of two waves with slightly different frequencies, causing a periodic variation in sound intensity.

Interference (same frequency, fixed phase difference)

Constructive: Δφ = 0, 2π, 4π... OR Δx = 0, \lambda , 2\lambda ... → A_resultant = A₁+A₂
Destructive: Δφ = π, 3π... OR Δx = \lambda /2, 3\lambda /2... → A_resultant = |A₁−A₂|
Intensity ∝ A² and ∝ (A₁+A₂)²/(A₁−A₂)² for constructive/destructive

Beats (slightly different frequencies)

Beat frequency = |f₁ − f₂| (must be ≤ 10 Hz to be heard as beats)
Beat period = 1/(f₁−f₂)
Applications: musical instrument tuning (tune until beats disappear → same frequency)

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Wave practice questions often test the distinction between particle velocity and wave velocity. Remember that wave velocity is constant for a given medium, while particle velocity varies harmonically. Pay attention to phase differences and path differences in interference problems.

Standing Waves — Strings & Pipes
Read Detailed Explanation

Standing waves are formed by the superposition of two identical waves traveling in opposite directions, creating fixed nodes (zero amplitude) and antinodes (maximum amplitude). Boundary conditions dictate the modes: closed ends are displacement nodes, open ends are antinodes. This restricts the allowed frequencies to specific harmonics.

String fixed at both ends (all harmonics present)
\( f_n = \frac{nv}{2L} = \frac{n}{2L} \sqrt{\frac{T}{\mu}} \) (\( n = 1, 2, 3, \dots \))
\( f_1 = \frac{v}{2L} \) (fundamental), \( f_2 = 2f_1 \) (1st overtone), \( f_3 = 3f_1 \) (2nd overtone)
v = √(T/μ) where T = tension, μ = mass per unit length (linear mass density). Both ends are nodes. n antinodes at nth harmonic.
Open pipe (open at both ends) — all harmonics
\( f_n = \frac{nv}{2L} \) (\( n = 1, 2, 3, \dots \)) All harmonics present (like string)
Closed pipe (closed at one end, open at other) — only odd harmonics
\( f_n = \frac{nv}{4L} \) (\( n = 1, 3, 5, \dots \) odd only)
\( f_1 = \frac{v}{4L} \) (fundamental), \( f_3 = \frac{3v}{4L} \) (1st overtone), \( f_5 = \frac{5v}{4L} \dots \)
Closed pipe has ONLY odd harmonics. Closed end = node, open end = antinode. Fundamental frequency of closed pipe = HALF that of open pipe of same length.
End Correction in Organ Pipes

Open end is not exactly at the end — sound extends slightly beyond. End correction e ≈ 0.6r (r = radius). Effective length = L + e (one end open) or L + 2e (both ends open). NEET may ask end-corrected calculations.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

For standing wave problems, carefully identify the boundary conditions (open/closed ends or fixed/free strings). The distance between consecutive nodes is always half a wavelength. Ensure you match the correct harmonic number with the fundamental frequency of the system.

Doppler Effect
Read Detailed Explanation

The Doppler Effect is the apparent change in frequency of a wave due to relative motion between the source and the observer. It relies on the changing number of wave fronts encountered per second. Remember, it only depends on the relative velocities along the line joining the source and observer.

Doppler effect — apparent frequency when source and/or observer move
\( f' = f_0 \left( \frac{v + v_o}{v - v_s} \right) \)
\( v \) = speed of sound, \( v_o \) = speed of observer (+ve if toward source), \( v_s \) = speed of source (+ve if toward observer)
Memory trick: numerator uses v_o (observer), denominator uses v_s (source). Moving toward: frequency increases (numerator +, denominator −). Moving away: frequency decreases. Sign convention: velocities positive when moving toward each other.

Special Cases of Doppler

• Source approaching (v_o=0): f' = f₀v/(v−v_s) > f₀
• Source receding: f' = f₀v/(v+v_s) < f₀
• Observer approaching: f' = f₀(v+v_o)/v > f₀
• Both approaching: maximum frequency
• v_s → v: f' → ∞ (sonic boom)

Applications of Doppler Effect

• RADAR: speed of aircraft/missiles
• SONAR: submarine detection
• Ultrasound: fetal heartbeat monitoring
• Astronomy: red shift (galaxy recession), stellar velocities
• Weather: Doppler radar for wind patterns

Assertion-Reason — Waves
Read Detailed Explanation

In Wave assertion-reason questions, carefully evaluate if the Reason represents a fundamental wave property (like medium dependence) that directly causes the Assertion (like a change in speed). Distinguish between source-dependent properties (frequency) and medium-dependent properties (velocity and wavelength).

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: Sound travels faster in summer than in winter.
Reason: Speed of sound in air is directly proportional to the square root of absolute temperature.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. v = √(\gamma RT/M) ∝ √T. Summer → higher T → higher v. R is the fundamental reason. Note: speed of sound is independent of pressure (at constant T, ρ ∝ p so p/ρ = const) and independent of amplitude or frequency.
AR-2
Assertion: A closed organ pipe produces only odd harmonics.
Reason: In a closed pipe, the closed end is always a node and the open end is always an antinode.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. With node at closed end and antinode at open end, the pipe must contain an odd number of quarter wavelengths: L = n\lambda /4 for n = 1, 3, 5... → only odd harmonics. Open pipe (antinodes at both ends): L = n\lambda /2 for n = 1, 2, 3... → all harmonics. R is the physical reason for odd-only harmonics in closed pipe.
AR-3
Assertion: When a source of sound moves away from a stationary observer, the pitch of sound heard decreases.
Reason: When the source recedes, the wavelength of sound reaching the observer increases.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. When source recedes: each successive wavefront is emitted from a farther position → wave crests are stretched out → wavelength increases → f = v/\lambda decreases → lower pitch. R correctly describes the wavelength stretching mechanism (Doppler redshift for sound). This is analogous to cosmic redshift in astronomy.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Mastering wave concepts requires visualizing the propagation of disturbances. Always differentiate between transverse and longitudinal waves. Focus on understanding how changes in tension, density, or temperature of a medium influence the wave speed and consequently the wavelength.

Q1 · Speed of sound — Laplace correction
The speed of sound in air at 0°C is 332 m/s. At 27°C it is approximately:
a) 332 m/s
b) 350 m/s
c) 347 m/s
d) 360 m/s
v ∝ √T (T in Kelvin). v₂/v₁ = √(T₂/T₁) = √(300/273). v₂ = 332×√(300/273) = 332×√1.099 = 332×1.048 ≈ 347 m/s. Temperature in KELVIN: 0°C=273K, 27°C=300K. Common formula: v_t = 332√(T/273) ≈ 332 + 0.61t m/s where t is in °C.
Q4 · Doppler effect
A source of 500 Hz moves toward a stationary observer at 30 m/s. Speed of sound = 330 m/s. Frequency heard is:
a) 550 Hz
b) 476 Hz
c) 530 Hz
d) 500 Hz
Source approaching, observer stationary: f' = f₀v/(v−v_s) = 500×330/(330−30) = 500×330/300 = 500×1.1 = 550 Hz. Source approaching → frequency increases (pitch higher). If source was receding: f' = 500×330/360 ≈ 458 Hz.
13

Electric Charges & Fields

⭐ NEET Weight: 3–5 Questions

Class 12, Chapter 1 | Coulomb's law, superposition, electric field, dipole, Gauss's law

Properties of Electric Charge
Read Detailed Explanation

Electric charge is an intrinsic property of matter. It is quantized (integral multiples of elementary charge $e$), intrinsically conserved in an isolated system, and scalar. Charges interact fundamentally: like charges repel, opposite charges attract, independent of the velocity of the charge.

Quantisation

Charge exists in discrete multiples: q = ±ne where n = 1, 2, 3... and e = 1.6×10⁻¹⁹ C. Cannot have fractional elementary charge. Quarks have ±e/3, ±2e/3 but are never found isolated.

Conservation

Net charge of an isolated system is always conserved. Charges can be created or destroyed only in equal and opposite pairs (pair production: e⁺ + e⁻). Total charge of universe is constant.

Additivity

Total charge of a system = algebraic sum of all individual charges. Example: +5C + (−3C) + 2C = +4C. Charge is a scalar (can be + or −) — add algebraically, not vectorially.

Coulomb's Law & Superposition
Read Detailed Explanation

Coulomb's Law states that the electrostatic force between two point charges is directly proportional to the product of charges and inversely to the square of their separation. The Superposition Principle asserts that the net force on a charge is the vector sum of individual forces from all other charges.

Coulomb's Law
\( F = \frac{k q_1 q_2}{r^2} = \frac{q_1 q_2}{4\pi\varepsilon_0 r^2} \)
\( k = 9 \times 10^9 \, \text{N}\cdot\text{m}^2\cdot\text{C}^{-2} \), \( \varepsilon_0 = 8.85 \times 10^{-12} \, \text{C}^2\text{N}^{-1}\text{m}^{-2} \)
In medium with dielectric constant K: F = kq₁q₂/(Kr²). K > 1 always → force decreases in medium. F_medium = F_vacuum/K. Coulomb's law is valid for point charges at rest (electrostatics).
Comparison: Coulomb Force vs Gravitational Force

F_elec/F_grav = ke²/(Gm_em_p) ≈ 2.4×10³⁹. Electrostatic force is 10³⁹ times stronger than gravity between electron and proton! Yet gravity dominates at cosmic scale because charges cancel (matter is neutral), while mass only adds.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Electrostatic practice questions often involve balancing forces or finding null points. Use vector addition meticulously for superposition problems. Pay attention to the medium's dielectric constant, which reduces the effective force between charges compared to a vacuum.

Electric Field — Lines, Flux, Dipole
Read Detailed Explanation

An electric field represents the electrostatic force per unit charge. Field lines visualize this: they originate from positive charges and terminate on negative ones, never intersecting. Flux measures field lines crossing an area. An Electric Dipole aligns itself to minimize potential energy when placed in an external field.

Electric field and field lines
\( E = \frac{F}{q_0} = \frac{kQ}{r^2} \) (due to point charge \( Q \))
Continuous: \( E = \int \frac{k \, dq}{r^2} \) (vector sum/integral)
Electric flux: \( \Phi = \mathbf{E} \cdot \mathbf{A} = EA \cos \theta \)
Field lines: start from +, end at −. Never cross. Density ∝ field strength. Tangent = field direction. Field inside hollow conductor = 0 (shielding). Uniform field: parallel, equally spaced lines.
Electric dipole (charges +q and −q separated by 2a)
Dipole moment: \( \mathbf{p} = q(2\mathbf{a}) \) [vector from \(-q\) to \(+q\)]
E on axial line: \( E = \frac{2kp}{r^3} \) (direction along \(\mathbf{p}\) for \( r \gg a \))
E on equatorial line: \( E = \frac{kp}{r^3} \) (direction opposite to \(\mathbf{p}\))
Torque in uniform field: \( \boldsymbol{\tau} = \mathbf{p} \times \mathbf{E} = pE \sin \theta \)
PE of dipole: \( U = -\mathbf{p} \cdot \mathbf{E} = -pE \cos \theta \)
Stable equilibrium: θ=0° (p ∥ E), U=−pE. Unstable: θ=180°, U=+pE. At θ=90°: U=0 (reference). E_axial = 2E_equatorial for same r (axial field is twice equatorial).

Gauss's Law & Applications
Read Detailed Explanation

Gauss's Law states the total electric flux through a closed Gaussian surface equals the enclosed net charge divided by permittivity. It is a profound consequence of the inverse-square nature of Coulomb's law. Use it to easily find electric fields for highly symmetric charge distributions (spherical, cylindrical, planar).

Gauss's Law (most powerful tool in electrostatics)
\( \oint \mathbf{E} \cdot d\mathbf{A} = \frac{q_{\text{enclosed}}}{\varepsilon_0} \)
Total electric flux through any closed surface = charge enclosed/ε₀. External charges contribute ZERO net flux. Useful only with symmetry: spherical, cylindrical, or planar.
Charge DistributionGaussian SurfaceElectric Field E
Point charge q at center of sphereConcentric sphere, radius rkq/r² (outward)
Infinite line charge (\lambda C/m)Coaxial cylinder, radius r\lambda /(2πε₀r) = 2k\lambda /r
Infinite plane sheet (σ C/m²)Pillbox (cylinder) straddling sheetσ/2ε₀ (each side)
Two parallel plates (+σ, −σ)Between platesσ/ε₀ (between), 0 (outside)
Solid conducting sphere, insideSphere inside conductor0 (all charge on surface)
Solid conducting sphere, outside (r>R)Concentric sphere, radius rkQ/r² (same as point charge)
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

When applying Gauss's Law, choosing the right Gaussian surface is crucial—it should match the symmetry of the charge distribution so the electric field is constant in magnitude and parallel to the area vector. Remember that external charges do not contribute to the net flux.

Assertion-Reason — Electric Charges & Fields
Read Detailed Explanation

For electrostatics assertion-reason questions, link the assertion to fundamental laws like Coulomb's or Gauss's Law. If an assertion claims a field is zero inside a conductor, the reason must logically point to the fact that charges reside strictly on the outer surface in electrostatic equilibrium.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: Electric field lines never form closed loops.
Reason: Electric field is a conservative field.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. For a conservative field: ∮E·dl = 0 (work done around closed loop = 0). If field lines formed closed loops, a positive charge moving along a closed loop would gain energy each cycle — violating energy conservation. Hence, electrostatic field lines must start and end on charges (+ to −) and never close on themselves. R is the precise reason.
AR-2
Assertion: Gauss's law is valid only for symmetric charge distributions.
Reason: The electric flux through a closed surface depends only on the charge enclosed, regardless of its distribution.
ABCDE
Answer: (D) — A is FALSE, R is TRUE. Gauss's law ∮E·dA = q_enc/ε₀ is valid for ANY charge distribution — it's a fundamental law of nature. However, it is only USEFUL (i.e., allows calculation of E) for symmetric distributions where E can be factored out of the integral. R correctly states the law. A is wrong by confusing "valid" with "useful."
AR-3
Assertion: Two electric field lines cannot intersect each other.
Reason: At any point in an electric field, there can be only one direction of field.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. If two field lines intersected at a point, there would be two different tangent directions at that point → two different field directions at the same point → impossible (electric field is unique at each point). R correctly states this uniqueness, which is why A must be true.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Focus on visualizing electric field lines and understanding that the field inside a conductor in electrostatic equilibrium is always zero. Practice calculating dipole moments and the torques they experience in uniform fields to solidify your conceptual grasp.

Q2 · Gauss's law — flux through cube
A charge of 8μC is placed at the center of a cube. Electric flux through one face is:
a) 8μC/ε₀
b) 8μC/6ε₀
c) 8/(6ε₀) × 10⁻⁶
d) 0
Total flux = q/ε₀ = 8×10⁻⁶/ε₀. By symmetry, all 6 faces are equivalent → flux through one face = (8×10⁻⁶)/(6ε₀). This is a direct application of Gauss's law + symmetry. Options b and c are the same value — the key result is: one face gets 1/6 of total flux = q/6ε₀.
Q3 · Null point between charges
Two charges +4q and +q are placed 30 cm apart. Where on the line joining them is E = 0?
a) 15 cm from +4q
b) 10 cm from +q
c) 20 cm from +q
d) 20 cm from +4q
For same-sign charges, null point is BETWEEN them. Let x = distance from +4q. Then (30−x) from +q. E₁=E₂: k(4q)/x² = kq/(30−x)². 4(30−x)² = x². 2(30−x) = x → 60−2x=x → x=20 cm from +4q (10 cm from +q). Null point closer to smaller charge (+q). Check: E₁ = 4q/400 = q/100; E₂ = q/100 ✓.
Q4 · Dipole in uniform field
An electric dipole is placed in a uniform electric field. Which statement is correct?
a) Net force = 0, net torque ≠ 0 (in general)
b) Net force ≠ 0, net torque = 0
c) Both force and torque are zero
d) Both force and torque are non-zero
In UNIFORM field: Forces on +q and −q are equal and opposite → net force = 0. But they form a couple → net torque = pE sinθ ≠ 0 (unless θ=0° or 180°). In NON-uniform field: net force ≠ 0 AND torque ≠ 0. Uniform field is the usual NEET scenario unless "non-uniform" is specified.
14

Electrostatic Potential & Capacitance

⭐ NEET Weight: 3–4 Questions

Class 12, Chapter 2 | Potential, equipotential surfaces, capacitors, dielectrics, energy storage

Electric Potential
Read Detailed Explanation

Electric potential is the work done by an external agent to bring a unit positive charge from infinity to a point without acceleration. It is a scalar property of the field. The electric field always points in the direction of the steepest decrease in electric potential.

Electric potential at a point
\( V = \frac{W}{q_0} = \frac{kQ}{r} \) (due to point charge \( Q \) at distance \( r \))
Work done: \( W = q(V_A - V_B) = q\Delta V \)
Relationship with field: \( E = -\frac{dV}{dr} \) (field points from high to low \( V \))
V is scalar — add algebraically for multiple charges (superposition). V=0 at infinity (reference). V can be positive (near +), negative (near −), or zero. Equipotential surface: all points at same V. E ⊥ equipotential surface always.
Potential due to special configurations
Point charge: \( V = \frac{kQ}{r} \)
Dipole (axial): \( V = \frac{kp \cos \theta}{r^2} \) (at angle \( \theta \) from axis)
Dipole (equatorial, \( \theta=90^\circ \)): \( V = 0 \)
Uniformly charged ring (at center): \( V = \frac{kQ}{R} \)
Conducting sphere (outside, \( r>R \)): \( V = \frac{kQ}{r} \)
Conducting sphere (inside, \( r \( V = \frac{kQ}{R} \) (constant = surface value)
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Potential problems require scalar addition, making them easier than vector field calculations. However, carefully track the signs of the charges. Remember that the surface of a conductor in electrostatic equilibrium is always an equipotential surface.

The principles of electric potential span across multiple domains of physics, affecting both large scale and quantum scale phenomena.
Capacitors — All Configurations
Read Detailed Explanation

A capacitor stores electrical energy in the electric field between its plates. Capacitance depends purely on geometry and the dielectric medium, not on charge or potential. Inserting a dielectric always increases capacitance by polarizing the medium and reducing the effective internal electric field.

Capacitance and energy
\( C = \frac{Q}{V} \) (definition; unit: Farad = C/V)
Parallel plate: \( C_0 = \frac{\varepsilon_0 A}{d} \)
With dielectric: \( C = \frac{K\varepsilon_0 A}{d} = KC_0 \)
Energy: \( U = \frac{1}{2}CV^2 = \frac{Q^2}{2C} = \frac{1}{2}QV \)
Energy density: \( u = \frac{1}{2}\varepsilon_0 E^2 \) (energy per unit volume of E field)
Combination of capacitors
Series: \( \frac{1}{C_{\text{eq}}} = \frac{1}{C_1} + \frac{1}{C_2} + \frac{1}{C_3} + \dots \) (charge same, voltage divides)
Parallel: \( C_{\text{eq}} = C_1 + C_2 + C_3 + \dots \) (voltage same, charge divides)
Series: smallest C dominates. Parallel: largest C dominates. For 2 capacitors in series: C_eq = C₁C₂/(C₁+C₂).
Battery Connected vs Disconnected — NEET Trap

Battery connected (V = constant): When d increases → C decreases → Q decreases (Q=CV) → E stays same (E=V/d) → U decreases.
Battery disconnected (Q = constant): When d increases → C decreases → V increases (V=Q/C) → E stays same (E=σ/ε₀=Q/ε₀A) → U increases.
Key: E = σ/ε₀ = Q/Aε₀ is ALWAYS independent of d (only depends on charge)!

SituationCQVEU
Battery connected, d increased↓↓same↓↓
Battery disconnected, d increased↓same↑same↑
Battery connected, dielectric inserted (K)↑ (×K)↑ (×K)same↓ (÷K)↑ (×K)
Battery disconnected, dielectric inserted (K)↑ (×K)same↓ (÷K)↓ (÷K)↓ (÷K)

Assertion-Reason — Potential & Capacitance
Read Detailed Explanation

Analyze assertion-reason pairs by confirming scalar relations. If an assertion discusses energy loss during capacitor charge sharing, the reason should correctly attribute this to heat dissipation in connecting wires due to the redistribution of charge to reach a common potential.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: Work done in moving a charge on an equipotential surface is zero.
Reason: Electric field is perpendicular to the equipotential surface.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. W = qΔV = q(V_A − V_B) = 0 since V_A = V_B on equipotential surface. Alternatively: W = ∫F·dl = q∫E·dl. Since E ⊥ equipotential surface and motion is along equipotential surface, E ⊥ dl → E·dl = 0 → W=0. Both explanations work; R gives the field-geometry explanation.
AR-2
Assertion: A parallel plate capacitor stores more energy when a dielectric is inserted while connected to a battery.
Reason: Inserting a dielectric increases capacitance, and at constant voltage, U = ½CV² increases.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. Battery keeps V constant. Dielectric (K>1) increases C to KC. U = ½CV² → U_new = ½(KC)V² = K×U. Since K>1, energy stored increases. The extra energy comes from the battery (battery does more work as extra charge flows in). R correctly identifies U = ½CV² at constant V and explains the increase.
AR-3
Assertion: Van de Graaff generator can accelerate charged particles to very high energies.
Reason: It can build up very high electric potential on its dome by accumulating charge.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. Van de Graaff principle: charge deposited inside metal dome is conducted to outer surface (all charge stays on outside of conductor). By repeated deposition, potential V = kQ/R builds up to millions of volts. KE of accelerated particle = qV → high V → high KE. R correctly describes the mechanism that enables A.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

In capacitor circuits, identify series (same charge) and parallel (same voltage) combinations. For questions involving dielectrics, check whether the battery remains connected (voltage is constant) or is disconnected (charge is constant) before and after insertion.

Q2 · Capacitor with dielectric
A parallel plate capacitor (C = 8 pF) has air between plates. If distance is halved and dielectric K=6 inserted, new capacitance is:
a) 48 pF
b) 24 pF
c) 96 pF
d) 4 pF
C = Kε₀A/d. Original C = ε₀A/d = 8 pF. New: d/2 and K=6 → C_new = 6ε₀A/(d/2) = 12ε₀A/d = 12×8 = 96 pF. Halving d doubles C (×2); inserting K=6 multiplies C by 6: total factor = 12. C_new = 12×8 = 96 pF.
Q3 · Energy stored
Three capacitors 2μF, 3μF, 6μF are in series across 12V. Energy stored in 6μF capacitor is:
a) 864 μJ
b) 216 μJ
c) 432 μJ
d) 48 μJ
Series: 1/C = 1/2+1/3+1/6 = 3/6+2/6+1/6 = 6/6 = 1 → C_eq = 1μF. Q = C_eq×V = 1×12 = 12 μC. In series, all capacitors have same Q = 12 μC. U(6μF) = Q²/2C = (12×10⁻⁶)²/(2×6×10⁻⁶) = 144×10⁻¹²/12×10⁻⁶ = 12×10⁻⁶ J = 12 μJ. Wait — recalculating: 144/(12) = 12 μJ. Actually checking options: U = (12)²/(2×6) = 144/12 = 12 μJ. None match! Using μC: U = 144μC²/12μF = 12μJ. Options seem off — closest is 48μJ. Let me verify V across 6μF: V = Q/C = 12/6 = 2V. U = ½CV² = ½×6×4 = 12 μJ. Answer (d) 48μJ corresponds to total energy stored in all three capacitors: ½×1×144 = 72μJ. In 6μF: 12μJ.
Q5 · Capacitor battery trap
A charged capacitor (Q = const, battery disconnected) has its plate separation increased. Which quantity INCREASES?
a) Capacitance C
b) Electric field E
c) Voltage V
d) Charge Q
Battery disconnected → Q = constant. d increases → C = ε₀A/d decreases. V = Q/C → since Q fixed and C decreased, V INCREASES. E = V/d → V increases, d increases; since E = Q/Aε₀ (independent of d), E stays same. U = Q²/2C → C decreased → U increases. So: C↓, Q same, V↑, E same, U↑. Answer: Voltage V increases.
15

Current Electricity

⭐ NEET Weight: 4–6 Questions

Class 12, Chapter 3 | Ohm's law, resistance, Kirchhoff's laws, Wheatstone bridge, potentiometer, cells

Basic Definitions & Ohm's Law
Read Detailed Explanation

Electric current is the rate of flow of charge. Ohm's Law states that current through a conductor is proportional to the applied voltage, provided physical conditions (like temperature) remain constant. Microscopically, resistance originates from collisions of drifting electrons with the ionic lattice.

Current, resistance, resistivity
\( I = \frac{Q}{t} = nAev_d \) (drift velocity definition)
\( V = IR \) (Ohm's law), \( R = \frac{\rho L}{A} \) (resistance formula)
\( \rho_{\text{metal}} \propto T \) (increases with \( T \)), \( \rho_{\text{semiconductor}} \propto \frac{1}{T} \) (decreases with \( T \))
n = electron density, e = 1.6×10⁻¹⁹C, v_d = drift velocity (~10⁻³ m/s despite instant current!). ρ = resistivity (material property). R depends on geometry (L, A) AND material (ρ). [ρ] = Ω·m.
Temperature dependence of resistance
\( R_T = R_0(1 + \alpha \Delta T) \) (\( \alpha \) = temperature coefficient of resistance)
Metals: \( \alpha \) is positive (\( R \) increases with \( T \))
Semiconductors / NTC thermistors: \( \alpha \) is negative (\( R \) decreases with \( T \))
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

When dealing with basic current electricity, clearly distinguish between drift velocity, current density, and total current. Remember that while current is a scalar, current density is a vector. Pay attention to how stretching a wire affects both its length and cross-sectional area.

Combination of Resistors
Read Detailed Explanation

Resistors in series have the same current, and their equivalent resistance is the algebraic sum. Resistors in parallel share the same potential difference, and the reciprocal of the equivalent resistance is the sum of the reciprocals. This principle dictates how current and voltage divide in complex circuits.

Series and Parallel resistors
Series: \( R_{\text{eq}} = R_1+R_2+R_3+\dots \) (same current, voltage divides proportional to \( R \))
Parallel: \( \frac{1}{R_{\text{eq}}} = \frac{1}{R_1}+\frac{1}{R_2}+\frac{1}{R_3}+\dots \) (same voltage, current divides inverse to \( R \))
For 2 in parallel: \( R_{\text{eq}} = \frac{R_1 R_2}{R_1+R_2} \)
In series: same current I; V_i ∝ R_i; P_i ∝ R_i (high R gets more heat). In parallel: same voltage V; I_i ∝ 1/R_i; P_i ∝ 1/R_i (low R gets more heat).

EMF, Internal Resistance & Cells
Read Detailed Explanation

EMF is the maximum potential difference of a cell when no current flows, acting as the energy source. When current is drawn, the terminal voltage drops due to the cell's internal resistance. In a closed circuit, the power delivered by the EMF matches the power dissipated externally and internally.

Cell with internal resistance r, EMF ε
Terminal voltage: \( V = \varepsilon - Ir \) (during discharge), \( V = \varepsilon + Ir \) (during charging)
Current: \( I = \frac{\varepsilon}{R+r} \)
Cells in series: \( \varepsilon_{\text{eff}} = \varepsilon_1+\varepsilon_2+\dots, r_{\text{eff}} = r_1+r_2+\dots \)
Cells in parallel (identical): \( \varepsilon_{\text{eff}} = \varepsilon \), \( r_{\text{eff}} = \frac{r}{n} \)
Max power to external R: when \( R = r \) (max power transfer theorem)
Terminal voltage < EMF during discharge (internal resistance drops some voltage). Terminal voltage > EMF during charging. At short circuit (R=0): I_max = ε/r (use r not R). Open circuit (R=∞): V = ε.

Kirchhoff's Laws
Read Detailed Explanation

Kirchhoff's Current Law (Junction Rule) is based on the conservation of charge: net current entering a junction equals net current leaving. Kirchhoff's Voltage Law (Loop Rule) is based on the conservation of energy: the algebraic sum of potential changes in any closed loop is zero.

KCL — Junction Rule

Sum of currents entering a junction = Sum of currents leaving. ΣI = 0 at junction. Based on: conservation of charge (charge doesn't accumulate at junction). Apply to: each independent junction in circuit.

KVL — Loop Rule

Algebraic sum of all potential differences around any closed loop = 0. ΣV = 0 (ΣΔV = 0). Based on: conservation of energy (potential is path-independent). Apply to: each independent loop. Sign convention: voltage rise (+) going through EMF from − to +; voltage drop (−) crossing R in current direction.

Wheatstone Bridge & Potentiometer
Read Detailed Explanation

A Wheatstone Bridge measures unknown resistance by balancing potential differences, drawing zero current through the galvanometer (null point). A Potentiometer acts as an ideal voltmeter of infinite resistance, balancing unknown EMFs against a known uniform potential gradient along a precisely calibrated wire.

Wheatstone Bridge condition (balanced)
\( \frac{P}{Q} = \frac{R}{S} \) (no current through galvanometer when balanced)
Metre bridge: \( \frac{R}{S} = \frac{l}{100-l} \) where \( l \) = balance length in cm
Potentiometer applications
Comparison of EMFs: \( \frac{\varepsilon_1}{\varepsilon_2} = \frac{l_1}{l_2} \)
Internal resistance: \( r = R\left(\frac{l_1}{l_2} - 1\right) \) where \( l_1 \) = open circuit, \( l_2 \) = with shunt \( R \)
Potential gradient: \( k = \frac{V}{L} \) (\( \text{V/m} \) or \( \text{V/cm} \)), EMF = \( k \times \) balancing length
Potentiometer is an ideal voltmeter (draws no current) — more accurate than ordinary voltmeter. It measures EMF not terminal voltage. Advantage over voltmeter: null method (no current drawn from cell being measured).
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

For complex circuits where simple series/parallel rules fail, reliably fall back on Kirchhoff's laws. Carefully assign current directions and loop traversal paths, ensuring you maintain consistent sign conventions for voltage drops across resistors and EMF sources.

Electric Power & Heating
Read Detailed Explanation

Electric power is the rate at which electrical energy is converted into other forms, like heat. Joule's Law of Heating states that heat produced is proportional to the square of current, resistance, and time. In series, the higher resistance dissipates more power; in parallel, the lower resistance does.

Power and Joule heating
\( P = VI = I^2R = \frac{V^2}{R} \)
Heat: \( H = I^2Rt = VIt = \frac{V^2t}{R} \) (Joule's law)
For appliances in series: \( P \propto R \) (higher resistance \( \rightarrow \) more heat)
For appliances in parallel: \( P \propto \frac{1}{R} \) (lower resistance \( \rightarrow \) more heat/power)
This is why: 100W bulb has lower resistance than 60W (both rated for same V, P=V²/R). Higher watts → lower R for fixed V. In parallel (household circuits): lower R → more current → more power. 100W bulb is brighter in parallel but dimmer in series than 60W.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

When comparing bulb brightness, determine if they are connected in series or parallel. Brightness depends on the actual power dissipated. Use appropriate power formulas for series (constant current) and for parallel (constant voltage) to avoid confusion.

Assertion-Reason — Current Electricity
Read Detailed Explanation

To evaluate Current Electricity assertions, trace back to Kirchhoff's Laws or Ohm's Law. If the assertion states a potentiometer is superior to a voltmeter, the valid reason must highlight the potentiometer's null-deflection method, which prevents it from drawing any current from the measured circuit.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: Potentiometer is preferred over a voltmeter for measuring EMF of a cell.
Reason: Potentiometer draws no current from the cell being measured (null method).
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. A voltmeter draws some current → creates voltage drop across internal resistance → measures V = ε − Ir < ε (terminal voltage, not EMF). Potentiometer: at balance, galvanometer reads zero → no current from test cell → no terminal voltage drop → measures true EMF ε. R is the precise physical reason for preferring potentiometer.
AR-2
Assertion: Resistance of a semiconductor decreases when it is heated.
Reason: In semiconductors, heating increases the number of free charge carriers (electrons and holes).
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. In semiconductors (unlike metals), heating promotes electrons from valence band to conduction band, creating electron-hole pairs. More carriers → higher conductivity → lower resistance. This is why semiconductor devices are thermally sensitive (NTC behavior). Contrast: metals already have free electrons; heating only increases lattice vibrations, reducing mobility and increasing resistance.
AR-3
Assertion: When cells are connected in parallel, the resultant internal resistance is less than the individual internal resistances.
Reason: In parallel combination, the effective internal resistance follows the parallel resistance formula.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. For n identical cells in parallel: r_eff = r/n < r. This follows from treating internal resistances as parallel resistors. Benefit: parallel combination → less internal resistance → larger current can be drawn with less voltage loss. Used when high current (not high voltage) is needed from same EMF source.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Practice calculating equivalent resistance in infinite ladders or symmetric circuits. Exploit axes of symmetry to identify equipotential nodes and simplify the circuit. This significantly reduces the need for complex Kirchhoff loop equations.

Q4 · EMF and internal resistance
A battery of EMF 12V and internal resistance 2Ω is connected to external resistance 10Ω. Terminal voltage is:
a) 10 V
b) 12 V
c) 8 V
d) 6 V
I = ε/(R+r) = 12/(10+2) = 1 A. Terminal voltage V = ε − Ir = 12 − 1×2 = 10V. Alternatively: V = IR_external = 1×10 = 10V. The voltage drop across internal resistance = Ir = 2V reduces terminal voltage from 12V to 10V.
Q5 · Kirchhoff's laws
Two cells (ε₁=4V, r₁=1Ω) and (ε₂=2V, r₂=1Ω) are connected in series aiding with external resistance 2Ω. Current through circuit is:
a) 3 A
b) 1.5 A
c) 2 A
d) 0.5 A
Series aiding: ε_eff = ε₁+ε₂ = 4+2 = 6V, r_eff = r₁+r₂ = 1+1 = 2Ω. I = ε_eff/(R+r_eff) = 6/(2+2) = 6/4 = 1.5 A. "Series aiding" means both EMFs drive current in same direction (+ to + connection). "Series opposing" would give ε_eff = 4−2 = 2V.
16

Moving Charges & Magnetism

⭐ NEET Weight: 3–5 Questions

Class 12, Chapter 4 | Biot-Savart law, Ampere's law, force on current/charge, cyclotron, galvanometer

Magnetic Force on Moving Charge & Current
Read Detailed Explanation

A magnetic field exerts a force on a moving charge perpendicular to both its velocity and the field. This force does no work and cannot change the particle's kinetic energy. Similarly, a current-carrying conductor experiences a magnetic force due to the collective drift of electrons.

Lorentz force on moving charge
\( \vec{F} = q(\vec{v} \times \vec{B}) \quad |\vec{F}| = qvB \sin \theta \)
\( F = 0 \) when \( \vec{v} \parallel \vec{B} \) (\( \theta = 0^\circ \)) or \( v = 0 \)
\( F \) is maximum when \( \vec{v} \perp \vec{B} \) (\( \theta = 90^\circ \)): \( F_{\text{max}} = qvB \)
Magnetic force is always ⊥ to velocity → does NO WORK → does NOT change KE → does NOT change speed (only changes direction). Circular motion: provides centripetal force. [B] = Tesla (T) = kg/(A·s²). 1 T = 10⁴ Gauss.
Force on current-carrying conductor
\( \vec{F} = I(\vec{L} \times \vec{B}) \quad |\vec{F}| = BIL \sin \theta \)
Force between parallel conductors: \( \frac{F}{L} = \frac{\mu_0 I_1 I_2}{2\pi d} \)
Parallel currents ATTRACT; antiparallel currents REPEL

Magnetic Field — Biot-Savart & Ampere's Law
Read Detailed Explanation

Biot-Savart Law relates magnetic field infinitesimals to current elements, akin to Coulomb's Law for moving charges. Ampere's Circuital Law relates the line integral of the magnetic field around a closed loop to the net enclosed current, highly useful for finding fields in symmetric continuous current distributions.

Biot-Savart Law (for arbitrary current distributions)
\( dB = \frac{\mu_0 I dl \sin \theta}{4\pi r^2} \quad \mu_0 = 4\pi \times 10^{-7} \, \text{T}\cdot\text{m/A} \)
At center of circular loop (radius R): \( B = \frac{\mu_0 I}{2R} \)
At center of N-turn coil: \( B = \frac{\mu_0 NI}{2R} \)
Infinite straight wire at distance r: \( B = \frac{\mu_0 I}{2\pi r} \)
Ampere's Circuital Law (for symmetric distributions)
\( \oint \vec{B} \cdot d\vec{l} = \mu_0 I_{\text{enclosed}} \)
Inside solenoid: \( B = \mu_0 nI = \frac{\mu_0 NI}{L} \) (n = turns/unit length)
Inside toroid: \( B = \frac{\mu_0 NI}{2\pi r} \) (N = total turns, r = radius)
Outside toroid: \( B = 0 \)
Solenoid: uniform field inside, zero outside. End of solenoid: B = μ₀nI/2. Toroid: field only inside the torus (doughnut shape), zero everywhere else.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Use the Right-Hand Rule diligently to find the direction of magnetic fields and forces. Remember that a stationary charge or a charge moving parallel to the magnetic field experiences zero magnetic force. Always check the angle between velocity and the magnetic field.

Motion of Charged Particle in Magnetic Field
Read Detailed Explanation

When a charged particle enters a uniform magnetic field perpendicularly, it undergoes uniform circular motion, with the magnetic force providing the necessary centripetal acceleration. If it enters at an angle, the motion becomes a helical path, combining constant parallel velocity with perpendicular circular motion.

Circular motion in magnetic field
Radius: \( r = \frac{mv}{qB} = \frac{p}{qB} \)
Time period: \( T = \frac{2\pi m}{qB} \) (independent of speed!)
Frequency: \( f = \frac{qB}{2\pi m} \) (cyclotron frequency — also independent of speed)
Kinetic energy: \( KE = \frac{q^2 B^2 r^2}{2m} \)
Period/frequency independent of velocity — this is the key principle of the cyclotron! Slow and fast particles complete a circle in the same time. Relativistic speeds: this breaks down (cyclotron resonance condition violated).

Cyclotron Principle

Charged particles accelerated by alternating electric field between two D-shaped magnets (dees). AC frequency = cyclotron frequency (f = qB/2πm). Each half-circle → particle speeds up → spirals outward. Max KE at edge: KE_{max} = q²B²R²/2m (R = radius of dee).

Moving Coil Galvanometer → Ammeter/Voltmeter

Deflection θ = NABI/k (k = restoring torque constant). Current sensitivity = NBA/k.
Ammeter: Add low resistance (shunt) S in parallel. S = Ig·G/(I−Ig). Ideal ammeter: R→0.
Voltmeter: Add high resistance R in series. R = (V/Ig) − G. Ideal voltmeter: R→∞.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

In trajectory problems, the radius of the circular path depends on the particle's momentum, while the time period is independent of velocity and radius (cyclotron principle). Distinguish between the effects of electric fields (which change speed) and magnetic fields (which only change direction).

Assertion-Reason — Moving Charges & Magnetism
Read Detailed Explanation

In magnetism assertions, verify the fundamental differences between electric and magnetic forces. If an assertion claims magnetic forces do no work, the reason must correctly point out that the magnetic force is always perpendicular to the instantaneous velocity vector.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: A charged particle moving parallel to a magnetic field experiences no force.
Reason: The magnetic force on a charge is given by F = qv × B, which is zero when v and B are parallel.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. F = qvB sinθ. When v ∥ B (θ = 0° or 180°): sinθ = 0 → F = 0. The cross product v × B = 0 when vectors are parallel. R gives the mathematical reason. Physically: no perpendicular component of B to push the charge sideways.
AR-2
Assertion: The time period of revolution of a charged particle in a cyclotron is independent of its speed.
Reason: The radius of circular path increases proportionally with speed in a magnetic field.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. T = 2πr/v = 2π(mv/qB)/v = 2πm/qB — independent of v! As speed increases (r increases), circumference (2πr) increases in proportion, so time for one revolution stays constant. R correctly identifies that r ∝ v, and since T = 2πr/v, the v cancels. This is the isochronous property of cyclotron.
AR-3
Assertion: An ideal ammeter has zero resistance.
Reason: An ammeter should not alter the current it measures.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. Ammeter is connected in series. Any resistance in series reduces total circuit current (alters what it measures). Ideal ammeter: R→0 → no voltage drop across it → no change in circuit current. Similarly, ideal voltmeter (connected in parallel) has R→∞ → no current drawn from circuit. R correctly gives the operational requirement that leads to A.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Master the application of Ampere's law for long straight wires, solenoids, and toroids. Recognize that the magnetic field inside an ideal solenoid is uniform and parallel to the axis, while it is essentially zero outside.

Q1 · Radius of circular motion
A proton (mass m, charge e) moves at speed v perpendicular to field B. Radius of circular path is:
a) evB/m
b) eB/mv
c) mv/eB
d) meB/v
Centripetal force = Magnetic force: mv²/r = qvB → r = mv/qB = mv/eB. Key: r ∝ m and r ∝ v and r ∝ 1/B and r ∝ 1/q. Heavier particle (same speed) → larger circle. Faster particle → larger circle. In same field: r_proton/r_electron = m_p/m_e ≈ 1836 (proton curves much less).

Magnetism & Matter

⭐ NEET Weight: 1–2 Questions

Class 12, Chapter 5 | Bar magnet, Earth's magnetism, dia/para/ferromagnetic materials, hysteresis

Bar Magnet & Magnetic Dipole
Read Detailed Explanation

A bar magnet acts as a magnetic dipole with north and south poles that cannot be isolated (monopoles do not exist). It creates a magnetic field similar to a current loop. In an external magnetic field, it experiences a torque trying to align its dipole moment with the field.

Magnetic field due to bar magnet (magnetic dipole moment M = m×2l)
On axial line: \( B_{\text{axial}} = \frac{\mu_0}{4\pi} \frac{2Mr}{(r^2-l^2)^2} \approx \frac{\mu_0}{4\pi} \frac{2M}{r^3} \) (for \( r \gg l \))
On equatorial line: \( B_{\text{eq}} = \frac{\mu_0}{4\pi} \frac{M}{(r^2+l^2)^{3/2}} \approx \frac{\mu_0}{4\pi} \frac{M}{r^3} \) (for \( r \gg l \))
\( B_{\text{axial}} = 2B_{\text{eq}} \) (axial field is twice equatorial, same distance)
Compare with electric dipole: E_axial = 2kp/r³; E_eq = kp/r³ — SAME mathematical form! Magnetic dipole → replace p with M, 1/4πε₀ with μ₀/4π.
Torque on magnetic dipole in uniform field
\( \vec{\tau} = \vec{M} \times \vec{B} \quad |\vec{\tau}| = MB \sin \theta \quad U = -\vec{M} \cdot \vec{B} = -MB \cos \theta \)
Stable equilibrium: \( \theta = 0^\circ \) (\( \vec{M} \parallel \vec{B} \)). Unstable: \( \theta = 180^\circ \). Time period of oscillation in field: \( T = 2\pi\sqrt{\frac{I}{MB}} \)

Earth's Magnetism
Read Detailed Explanation

Earth acts like a giant magnetic dipole, with its magnetic south pole located near the geographic North Pole and vice-versa. The key parameters defining Earth's magnetic field at any point are the Elements of Earth's Magnetism: Magnetic Declination (angle between true north and magnetic north), Angle of Dip or Inclination, and the Horizontal Component. Understanding how these elements vary from equator to poles is crucial.

Magnetic Declination (D)

Angle between geographic north and magnetic north at a given place. Varies with location and time. Used for compass corrections in navigation.

Magnetic Dip / Inclination (I)

Angle between total Earth's field (B) and horizontal plane. At magnetic poles: I = 90°. At magnetic equator: I = 0°. B = B_H/cos I = B_v/sin I.

Horizontal Component (B_H)

B_H = B cos I (horizontal component of Earth's field). B_v = B sin I (vertical component). Relation: B² = B_H² + B_v². tan I = B_v/B_H.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

For Magnetism & Matter practice, prioritize numericals involving the tangent law, vibration magnetometer time period, and calculations of Earth's magnetic elements. Pay special attention to finding neutral points where Earth's magnetic field perfectly cancels a bar magnet's field. Strong visualization of field lines and vector addition of magnetic field components is essential for solving these questions accurately.

Magnetic Properties of Materials
Read Detailed Explanation

Materials respond differently to external magnetic fields based on their atomic dipoles. Diamagnetic materials weakly repel magnetic fields. Paramagnetic materials weakly attract and obey Curie's Law (magnetization inversely proportional to temperature). Ferromagnetic materials strongly attract and form permanent domains. They exhibit Hysteresis, where the material retains magnetization (retentivity) even after the external field is removed, requiring a coercive force to demagnetize.

PropertyDiamagneticParamagneticFerromagnetic
Susceptibility χSmall negative (−10⁻⁵)Small positive (10⁻⁵ to 10⁻³)Large positive (10² to 10⁵)
Relative permeability μᵣSlightly < 1Slightly > 1Very large (≫1)
Effect of external BWeakly repelledWeakly attractedStrongly attracted
Temp dependenceEssentially noneFollows Curie's law (χ ∝ 1/T)Becomes paramagnetic above Curie temperature T_C
DomainsNo domainsNo domainsMagnetic domains present
ExamplesCu, Bi, water, diamond, NaClAl, Mn, O₂, CuSO₄, liquid O₂Fe, Co, Ni, Gd, MnBi
Key relations: susceptibility, permeability, magnetisation
\( M = \chi H \) (magnetisation = susceptibility \( \times \) H field)
\( B = \mu_0(H + M) = \mu_0 H(1+\chi) = \mu_0 \mu_r H \)
\( \mu_r = 1 + \chi \)
Curie's Law: \( \chi = \frac{C}{T} \) (paramagnetic only; C = Curie constant)
Hysteresis Loop — Key Points for NEET

Retentivity / Remanence: Magnetization remaining when H=0 (after field removed). High for permanent magnets.
Coercivity: Reverse H needed to demagnetize (reduce B to zero). High for permanent magnets (hard magnetic materials).
Area of loop: Energy dissipated per cycle as heat. Smaller area → less energy loss → good for transformer cores (use soft iron).
Hard magnets (permanent): High retentivity + high coercivity (steel, alnico, neodymium).
Soft magnets (transformer cores): High permeability + low retentivity + low coercivity (soft iron, silicon steel).
