Assertion-Reason — Semiconductors
Read Detailed Explanation

Assertion-Reason questions delve into the fundamental physics of semiconductor behavior. For example, explaining why electron mobility intrinsically exceeds hole mobility, or why the depletion layer must widen under reverse bias. Accurate reasoning requires a deep understanding of the microscopic drift of carriers under electric fields versus the diffusion of carriers due to concentration gradients.

Options: (A) Both true, R explains A  |  (B) Both true, R doesn't explain A  |  (C) A true, R false  |  (D) A false, R true  |  (E) Both false

AR-1
Assertion: The resistance of a semiconductor decreases with increase in temperature.
Reason: In semiconductors, as temperature rises, more electron-hole pairs are thermally generated, increasing the number of charge carriers.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. In semiconductors: conductivity σ ∝ n_i ∝ e^(−E_g/2kT) (Boltzmann factor). Higher T → more carriers → higher σ → lower ρ → lower R. This is opposite to metals (where carriers are fixed; higher T → more scattering → higher R). R correctly identifies carrier generation as the mechanism. NTC (Negative Temperature Coefficient) thermistors exploit this property.
AR-2
Assertion: In a forward biased p-n junction, the depletion layer becomes narrower.
Reason: The applied forward bias opposes the built-in potential, reducing the electric field and the width of the depletion region.
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. Built-in field points from n to p (n is +, p is −). Forward bias applies external field from p to n (+ to p, − to n) — opposing built-in field. Net field decreases → depletion width decreases → barrier potential V_{bi} − V_forward → majority carriers can now flow → forward current. R correctly explains the mechanism: partial cancellation of built-in potential.
AR-3
Assertion: In a common emitter transistor amplifier, the output signal is 180° out of phase with the input signal.
Reason: Increasing base current increases collector current which increases voltage drop across collector resistance, decreasing collector voltage (V_CE decreases).
ABCDE
Answer: (A) — Both true, R explains A. When input V_BE increases (+ve half-cycle): I_B increases → I_C = \beta I_B increases → V_RC = I_C×R_C increases → V_CE = V_CC − V_RC decreases. So when input goes up, output (V_CE) goes down → 180° phase inversion. R traces the exact mechanism step by step. This phase inversion is a key property of the CE amplifier configuration.
Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Focus intensely on the unique operational characteristics of special-purpose diodes. Zener diodes are heavily doped to operate safely in reverse breakdown, providing vital voltage regulation. Photodiodes operate in reverse bias to linearly detect light intensity, while LEDs must be forward-biased to efficiently emit light through the recombination of electron-hole pairs.

Q2 · Transistor current gain
In a CE transistor circuit, I_B = 40 μA and \beta = 75. Collector current I_C is:
a) 0.53 mA
b) 40 μA
c) 3 mA
d) 75 mA
\beta = I_C/I_B → I_C = \beta × I_B = 75 × 40 μA = 3000 μA = 3 mA. A small base current (40 μA) controls a much larger collector current (3 mA) — this is current amplification. I_E = I_B + I_C = 40 + 3000 = 3040 μA ≈ 3.04 mA. The transistor amplifies current by factor \beta = 75.

Experimental Skills

⭐ NEET Weight: 2-3 Questions (Newly Added)

Class 11 & 12 | Vernier Callipers, Screw Gauge, Simple Pendulum, Optics, Electronics

Measurement Instruments
Read Detailed Explanation

Measurement instruments like the Vernier Caliper and Screw Gauge are vital for precise dimensions. The Least Count is the absolute smallest measurable value, dictating the instrument's precision. True accuracy demands identifying the Zero Error—a systematic flaw when jaws meet—which must be algebraically subtracted from the measured reading to find the true value.

Vernier Callipers

Least Count (LC): \( 1 \text{ MSD} - 1 \text{ VSD} \)
Reading: \( \text{MSR} + (\text{VSR} \times \text{LC}) \)
Zero Error: Positive if zero of vernier is to right of main scale zero. True Reading = Measured - Zero Error (with sign).

Screw Gauge

Pitch: Distance moved on linear scale per rotation.
Least Count: \( \frac{\text{Pitch}}{\text{Total circular divisions}} \)
Reading: \( \text{LSR} + (\text{CSR} \times \text{LC}) \)
Used for measuring diameter of thin wires or thickness of sheets.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Experimental questions rigorously test your ability to read scales and deduce the least count. For a Vernier Caliper, it is the theoretical difference between one Main Scale Division and one Vernier Scale Division. You must always critically account for positive or negative zero errors to correctly evaluate the final, true measurement.

Other Key Experiments
Read Detailed Explanation

Key laboratory experiments include determining focal lengths using an optical bench, finding the speed of sound via a resonance column, and measuring unknown resistances with a meter bridge. These rely on fundamental physics principles like standing wave resonance, balanced Wheatstone bridges, and thin lens equations, translating theoretical physics into measurable realities.

Simple Pendulum

Restoring force provides SHM. Graph of \( L \) vs \( T^2 \) is a straight line through origin. Slope = \( \frac{g}{4\pi^2} \). Used to determine \( g \).

Young's Modulus

Using Searle's apparatus. \( Y = \frac{FL}{A\Delta L} = \frac{MgL}{\pi r^2 l} \). Error in \( Y \) heavily depends on measurement of radius \( r \) since it's squared.

Viscosity & Surface Tension

Terminal Velocity: Sphere falling in liquid. \( v = \frac{2r^2(\rho-\sigma)g}{9\eta} \).
Capillary Rise: \( T = \frac{rh\rho g}{2\cos\theta} \). Added detergents decrease surface tension.

Concept Practice Question
Read Detailed Explanation

Questions on experimental skills highly assess error analysis and practical constraints. For instance, understanding why thick copper strips are used in a meter bridge (to minimize stray resistance), or how end correction critically affects resonance tube readings. A strong conceptual focus on the physical sources of systematic and random errors is vital.

Multi-Concept Questions

⭐ High-Value Integration Problems

Cross-chapter problems combining 2–3 concepts | NEET-level difficulty | Full solutions

Mechanics + Energy
Read Detailed Explanation

Multi-concept problems ingeniously blend kinematics, Newton's laws, and the Work-Energy Theorem. For example, analyzing a block sliding down a rough incline into a spring. You must simultaneously apply the grand conservation of mechanical energy while meticulously accounting for the negative work done by non-conservative forces, such as kinetic friction.

MQ1 · Projectile + Energy Conservation + Circular Motion
A ball is launched from the ground at 45° with speed 20 m/s. At the highest point, it enters a vertical circular loop of radius 2 m fixed at that height. (a) Find the speed of ball at highest point. (b) What is the minimum speed needed to complete the loop? (c) Does the ball complete the loop? (d) If not, what minimum launch speed is needed? (g = 10 m/s²)
(a) At highest point of projectile: only horizontal velocity remains. \( v_{top} = u \cos\theta = 20 \times \cos 45^\circ = 20 \times (1/\sqrt{2}) = 10\sqrt{2} \approx 14.14 \text{ m/s} \).

(b) For circular loop of radius \( R = 2 \text{ m} \), minimum speed at top of loop: \( v_{min} = \sqrt{gR} = \sqrt{10 \times 2} = \sqrt{20} = 2\sqrt{5} \approx 4.47 \text{ m/s} \). This is for top of the loop (height above entry = \( 2R = 4 \text{ m} \) above loop entry).

At loop bottom (entry): by energy conservation: \( \frac{1}{2}mv_{entry}^2 = \frac{1}{2}mv_{top}^2 + mg(2R) \). \( v_{entry}^2 = v_{top\_loop}^2 + 4gR \). \( v_{top\_loop\_min} = 2\sqrt{5} \text{ m/s} \rightarrow v_{entry\_min} = \sqrt{20 + 4 \times 10 \times 2} = \sqrt{20+80} = \sqrt{100} = 10 \text{ m/s} \).

(c) Ball enters loop at height \( H = u^2 \sin^2\theta / 2g = 400 \times 0.5 / 20 = 10 \text{ m} \) with speed \( 10\sqrt{2} \approx 14.14 \text{ m/s} > 10 \text{ m/s} \) (minimum needed). Yes, the ball completes the loop!

(d) Not needed since ball completes loop. If hypothetically it didn't: need \( u \cos\theta \ge 10 \rightarrow \text{for } \theta=45^\circ: u \ge 10/\cos 45^\circ = 10\sqrt{2} \approx 14.14 \text{ m/s} \). The given \( 20 \text{ m/s} \) gives \( 14.14 \text{ m/s} \rightarrow \) exactly sufficient.

Electrostatics + Current + Capacitance
Read Detailed Explanation

These challenging problems require tracing both charge and energy flow across domains. A classic example involves a capacitor discharging through a resistor, blending electrostatics with current electricity. You must conceptually understand that while capacitors act as open circuits to steady DC, they critically influence the transient time-dependent behavior of the entire circuit.

MQ2 · Capacitor Charging + RC Circuit + Energy
A capacitor (C = 10 μF) is initially uncharged. It is connected to a 100V battery through a resistance R = 1MΩ. (a) What is the initial current? (b) What is the time constant τ? (c) Find charge on capacitor after time 2τ. (d) What fraction of supplied energy is dissipated in R? (e) Final energy stored in capacitor?
(a) Initially capacitor is uncharged \( \rightarrow V_C = 0 \rightarrow \) all voltage across R. \( I_0 = V/R = 100/(10^6) = 10^{-4} \text{ A} = 0.1 \text{ mA} \).

(b) \( \tau = RC = 10^6 \times 10 \times 10^{-6} = 10 \text{ s} \).

(c) Charge at time t: \( Q(t) = CV(1 - e^{-t/\tau}) \). At \( t = 2\tau \): \( Q = 10 \times 10^{-6} \times 100 \times (1 - e^{-2}) = 10^{-3} \times (1 - 0.135) = 10^{-3} \times 0.865 = 0.865 \text{ mC} = 865 \text{ \mu C} \).

(d) This is an important result: regardless of R, exactly HALF the energy supplied by the battery is dissipated in R, and HALF is stored in C. Energy from battery: \( W = QV = CV^2 = 10 \times 10^{-6} \times (100)^2 = 0.1 \text{ J} \). Energy in C: \( \frac{1}{2}CV^2 = 0.05 \text{ J} \). Energy in R: \( 0.05 \text{ J} \) (exactly half). Fraction = \( 50\% \).

(e) Final energy in C \( = \frac{1}{2}CV^2 = \frac{1}{2} \times 10^{-5} \times 10^4 = 0.05 \text{ J} = 50 \text{ mJ} \). Note: This result (50% efficiency in RC charging) is universal — independent of R, V, C.

Optics + Modern Physics
Read Detailed Explanation

A common hybrid scenario involves photons interacting with a mirror or a macroscopic lens system. For instance, calculating the radiation pressure exerted by a light beam on a perfectly reflecting surface, or using the photoelectric effect to precisely determine the maximum kinetic energy of electrons emitted from a metallic focal point.

MQ3 · Photoelectric Effect + Hydrogen Spectrum + de Broglie
Ultraviolet radiation of wavelength 200 nm strikes a metal with work function 4 eV. (a) Calculate energy of incident photon. (b) Find maximum KE of emitted photoelectrons. (c) Find stopping potential. (d) Calculate de Broglie wavelength of the fastest photoelectron. (e) Which series of hydrogen spectrum lies in the same spectral region as this UV light? (h=6.63×10⁻³⁴ J·s, c=3×10⁸ m/s, m_e=9.1×10⁻³¹ kg)
(a) \( E = hc/\lambda = (6.63 \times 10^{-34} \times 3 \times 10^8)/(200 \times 10^{-9}) = 19.89 \times 10^{-26}/(2 \times 10^{-7}) = 9.945 \times 10^{-19} \text{ J} = 9.945 \times 10^{-19} / 1.6 \times 10^{-19} \approx 6.22 \text{ eV} \).

(b) \( KE_{max} = E - \phi = 6.22 - 4 = 2.22 \text{ eV} = 2.22 \times 1.6 \times 10^{-19} = 3.55 \times 10^{-19} \text{ J} \).

(c) Stopping potential \( V_s = KE_{max}/e = 2.22 \text{ V} \).

(d) de Broglie wavelength: \( p = \sqrt{2m_e \times KE_{max}} = \sqrt{2 \times 9.1 \times 10^{-31} \times 3.55 \times 10^{-19}} = \sqrt{6.46 \times 10^{-49}} = 8.04 \times 10^{-25} \text{ kg}\cdot\text{m/s} \). \( \lambda = h/p = 6.63 \times 10^{-34} / 8.04 \times 10^{-25} = 0.825 \times 10^{-9} \text{ m} \approx 0.82 \text{ nm} \). This is X-ray range! Interestingly, fast electrons have very short wavelengths.

(e) \( 200 \text{ nm} \) = UV range. Hydrogen Lyman series (\( n \rightarrow 1 \) transitions) spans 91–122 nm (far UV to near UV). Balmer series starts at 365 nm (visible). The 200 nm light is in far UV, corresponding to Lyman series region (specifically Lyman limit ~91nm to Lyman \( \alpha \) ~122nm). \( \lambda = 200 \text{ nm} \) is in the Lyman continuum region.

Thermodynamics + Kinetic Theory
Read Detailed Explanation

These questions masterfully mix macroscopic thermodynamic gas laws with microscopic kinetic particle behavior. You might need to directly relate the RMS speed of gas molecules to the macroscopic work done during an adiabatic expansion, or calculate the efficiency of a conceptual heat engine using the microscopic degrees of freedom of the gas.

MQ4 · Ideal Gas + Heat Engine + Degrees of Freedom
One mole of diatomic ideal gas (\gamma = 7/5) undergoes a Carnot cycle between 500 K and 300 K. (a) Maximum efficiency of the engine. (b) If 500 J of heat is absorbed per cycle, how much work is done? (c) Heat rejected to cold reservoir. (d) Change in internal energy during isothermal expansion at 500 K. (e) If gas is adiabatically compressed from 300 K to 500 K, find ratio V₁/V₂.
(a) \( \eta = 1 - T_C/T_H = 1 - 300/500 = 1 - 0.6 = 0.4 = 40\% \).

(b) \( W = \eta \times Q_H = 0.4 \times 500 = 200 \text{ J per cycle} \).

(c) \( Q_C = Q_H - W = 500 - 200 = 300 \text{ J} \) (or \( Q_C/Q_H = T_C/T_H = 300/500 = 0.6 \), so \( Q_C = 0.6 \times 500 = 300 \text{ J} \)).

(d) Isothermal process: \( T = \text{constant} \rightarrow \Delta U = nC_v\Delta T = 0 \) (for ideal gas). All absorbed heat = work done BY gas. \( \Delta U = 0 \). This confirms: in isothermal process of ideal gas, all Q converts to W.

(e) Adiabatic process: \( TV^{\gamma-1} = \text{constant} \). \( T_1V_1^{\gamma-1} = T_2V_2^{\gamma-1} \). \( 300 \times V_1^{2/5} = 500 \times V_2^{2/5} \). \( (V_1/V_2)^{2/5} = 500/300 = 5/3 \). \( V_1/V_2 = (5/3)^{5/2} = (5/3)^{2.5} = (1.667)^{2.5} \approx 3.6 \). So \( V_1 > V_2 \) — gas was compressed (\( V_1 \) is initial, \( V_2 \) is final; since T increases, volume must decrease in adiabatic compression).

a) Instrumental limitations

c) Environmental factors (e.g. friction/air resistance)
d) All of the above
Experimental setups for thermodynamics + kinetic theory are subject to all these standard sources of error, which must be accounted for in precise calculations.

a) It depends on the frame of reference

c) It is a fundamental property

Electromagnetism + Waves
Read Detailed Explanation

Hybrid questions frequently combine electromagnetic induction with simple harmonic motion—like a conducting rod oscillating within a uniform magnetic field. Alternatively, they may elegantly link the intrinsic properties of electromagnetic waves, such as intensity and energy density, with the macroscopic concepts of alternating current LC oscillations and circuit resonance.

MQ5 · LCR Circuit + EM Waves + Energy
An LCR series circuit has L=0.1H, C=100μF, R=20Ω connected to V=200sin(100t) V. (a) Find X_L and X_C at ω=100 rad/s. (b) Find impedance Z. (c) Is circuit above or below resonance? (d) Find resonance frequency. (e) Maximum current at resonance. (f) Q-factor of circuit.
(a) \( X_L = \omega L = 100 \times 0.1 = 10 \text{ \Omega} \). \( X_C = 1/\omega C = 1/(100 \times 100 \times 10^{-6}) = 1/(0.01) = 100 \text{ \Omega} \).

(b) \( Z = \sqrt{R^2 + (X_L - X_C)^2} = \sqrt{400 + (10 - 100)^2} = \sqrt{400 + 8100} = \sqrt{8500} \approx 92.2 \text{ \Omega} \).

(c) \( X_C > X_L \rightarrow \) circuit is CAPACITIVE \( \rightarrow \) below resonance (\( \omega < \omega_0 \)). Current leads voltage.

(d) \( \omega_0 = 1/\sqrt{LC} = 1/\sqrt{0.1 \times 10^{-4}} = 1/\sqrt{10^{-5}} = 1/(3.16 \times 10^{-3}) \approx 316.2 \text{ rad/s} \). \( f_0 = 316.2 / (2\pi) \approx 50.3 \text{ Hz} \).

(e) At resonance, \( Z = R = 20 \text{ \Omega} \). \( I_{0\_max} = V_0/R = 200/20 = 10 \text{ A} \). \( I_{rms} = 10/\sqrt{2} \approx 7.07 \text{ A} \).

(f) \( Q = \omega_0 L / R = 316.2 \times 0.1 / 20 = 31.62 / 20 \approx 1.58 \). Or \( Q = (1/R)\sqrt{L/C} = (1/20)\sqrt{0.1/10^{-4}} = (1/20)\sqrt{1000} = 31.62 / 20 \approx 1.58 \). This relatively low Q means broad resonance (not very selective).

Nuclear Physics + Radioactivity
Read Detailed Explanation

These intricate problems often connect the statistical nature of radioactive decay with classical kinematics or energy conservation. For example, you may be asked to calculate the exact recoil velocity of a heavy daughter nucleus following alpha emission, relying entirely on the strict conservation of linear momentum and the released nuclear Q-value.

MQ6 · Binding Energy + Decay Series + Fission Energy
²³⁸U undergoes alpha decay to ²³⁴Th, which then undergoes 2 beta-minus decays. (a) Write all three decay equations. (b) What is the daughter nucleus after all three decays? (c) If BE/nucleon of ²³⁸U is 7.57 MeV and that of ²³⁴Th is 7.59 MeV and ⁴He is 7.07 MeV, is the alpha decay energetically favorable? (d) Calculate Q-value in MeV. (e) If 1 kg of ²³⁸U undergoes complete decay series to Pb, what approximate mass converts to energy? (Nₐ = 6×10²³)

Nuclear Physics + Radioactivity
Read Detailed Explanation

Another challenging multi-concept angle involves strictly linking the decay activity of a radioactive sample with thermodynamics and calorimetry. For instance, determining the precise rise in temperature of a surrounding absorptive medium as it completely absorbs the kinetic energy of emitted alpha or beta particles over a specific period of time.

MQ6 · Binding Energy + Decay Series + Fission Energy
²³⁸U undergoes alpha decay to ²³⁴Th, which then undergoes 2 beta-minus decays. (a) Write all three decay equations. (b) What is the daughter nucleus after all three decays? (c) If BE/nucleon of ²³⁸U is 7.57 MeV and that of ²³⁴Th is 7.59 MeV and ⁴He is 7.07 MeV, is the alpha decay energetically favorable? (d) Calculate Q-value in MeV. (e) If 1 kg of ²³⁸U undergoes complete decay series to Pb, what approximate mass converts to energy? (Nₐ = 6×10²³)
(a) Alpha: \( ^{238}_{92}\text{U} \rightarrow \ ^{234}_{90}\text{Th} + \ ^4_2\text{He} \). Beta-1: \( ^{234}_{90}\text{Th} \rightarrow \ ^{234}_{91}\text{Pa} + e^- + \bar{\nu} \). Beta-2: \( ^{234}_{91}\text{Pa} \rightarrow \ ^{234}_{92}\text{U} + e^- + \bar{\nu} \).

(b) After all three: A=234, Z=92 \( \rightarrow \ ^{234}_{92}\text{U} \). This is an isotope of Uranium (same Z=92, different A). The decay series continues for millions of years to eventually reach stable \( ^{206}\text{Pb} \).

(c) Q-value for alpha decay: \( Q = BE(Th) + BE(He) - BE(U) \). \( Q = [234 \times 7.59 + 4 \times 7.07 - 238 \times 7.57] \text{ MeV} = [1776.06 + 28.28 - 1801.66] = 2.68 \text{ MeV} > 0 \). Positive Q \( \rightarrow \) exothermic \( \rightarrow \) energetically favorable. YES, alpha decay is spontaneous.

(d) \( Q \approx 2.68 \text{ MeV} \) (from above calculation). This energy appears as kinetic energy of alpha particle and recoiling Th nucleus. Since \( m_{He} \ll m_{Th} \), most KE goes to alpha particle.

(e) Q per alpha decay \( \approx 2.68 \text{ MeV} = 2.68 \times 1.6 \times 10^{-13} \text{ J} = 4.29 \times 10^{-13} \text{ J} \). Number of \( ^{238}\text{U} \) in 1 kg = \( N_A/238 = 6 \times 10^{23}/238 = 2.52 \times 10^{21} \) atoms. Total Q (alpha only) \( \approx 2.52 \times 10^{21} \times 4.29 \times 10^{-13} = 1.08 \times 10^9 \text{ J} \approx 1 \text{ GJ} \). Mass equivalent: \( m = E/c^2 = 10^9 / (9 \times 10^{16}) \approx 1.1 \times 10^{-8} \text{ kg} = 11 \text{ \mu g} \) (tiny but releases 1 GJ!).
d) Depends on the specific system
Conservation laws in nuclear physics + radioactivity strictly depend on whether the system is isolated, conservative, or non-conservative.
